The Traveling Imam's Announcement to the Congregation
Ruling on the recommendation for a traveling imam to announce his status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a traveling imam announcing his status, identify the relied-upon timing, and explain why it is recommended rather than obligatory.

Core Idea
The imam's travel announcement is sunnah, not wajib, and the best time is after both taslims.

When a traveling imam leads residents in prayer, should he inform them of his traveler status? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling: it is recommended (mandub), not obligatory, and the preferred timing is after completing the two taslims. We also explore a subtle anecdote from Imam Abu Yusuf that underscores the importance of not speaking unnecessarily in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling level
The act is the imam announcing he is a traveler. The Hanafi school classifies this as nadb (recommended), not wajib.
Nadb is synonymous with sunnah in the Hanafi school.
2
Note the ikhtilaf on timing
There are two views: after the first taslim or after both taslims. The text states 'fi al-ashah' indicating the relied-upon view is after both taslims.
The ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Understand why it is not obligatory
The announcement is not the only way for the congregation to know; they may ask before prayer or after completing their own prayer.
Obligation (wujub) would require that the imam's travel status cannot be known otherwise.
4
Identify the proper conduct (yambaghi)
It is proper for the imam to announce before starting the prayer to remove ambiguity from the beginning, even though the formal announcement after taslim is the recommended act.
This is a separate point of etiquette (adab).
5
Reflect on the anecdote of Abu Yusuf
The story shows that if the congregation already knows, there is no need to announce, and speaking in prayer is avoided unless necessary.
It underscores the prohibition of unnecessary speech in salah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونُدب للإمام بعد التسليمتين في الأصح، وقيل: بعد التسليمة الأولى أن يقول: أتموا صلاتكم فإني مسافر كما روينا، وإنما كان مندوباً لأنه لم يتعين معرفاً لحال الإمام لجواز السؤال قبل الصلاة أو بعد إتمامهم صلاتهم، وينبغي أن يقول لهم الإمام ذلك قبل شروعه في الصلاة؛ لدفع الاشتباه ابتداء. لطيفة: روي أن أبا يوسف لما حج مع هارون رحمهما الله وصلى بالناس ركعتين بمكة، ثم قال: أتموا يا أهل مكة صلاتكم، فإنا قوم سفر، فقال له واحد منهم: نحن أعلم بهذا منك، فقال أبو يوسف: لو علمت ما تكلمت في الصلاة، فقال هارون الرشيد: لو كان مثل هذا الجواب بدلاً عن ...
And it is recommended for the imam, after the two tashims on the most correct view, and it was said: after the first tashim, to say: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler,' as we have narrated. It is only recommended because it is not determined as a way to inform the congregation of the imam's state; it is permissible to ask before the prayer or after they complete their prayer. And it is proper for the imam to say that to them before commencing the prayer, to remove ambiguity from the beginning. A subtle point: It is narrated that when Abu Yusuf performed Hajj with Harun (may Allah have mercy on them) and led the people in two raka'at in Makkah, then he said: 'Complete, O people of Makkah, your prayer, for we are travelers.' One of them said: 'We know this better than you.' Abu Yusuf said: 'If I had known, I would not have spoken during the prayer.' Harun al-Rashid said: 'If only such a response were instead of...'
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage deals with the recommendation (nadb) for a traveling imam to inform his congregation that he is a traveler, so that they know to complete their prayer. The relied-upon view (al-ashah) is that he should say this after the two taslims (i.e., after completing his own prayer), though some say after the first taslim. The reason it is not obligatory is that the imam's state can be known by asking before prayer or after they complete. However, it is proper (yambaghi) for him to announce it before starting the prayer to avoid confusion from the beginning. The anecdote about Imam Abu Yusuf illustrates that he once announced after the prayer and was told they already knew, to which he responded that if he had known they knew, he would not have spoken during the prayer. This shows the importance of not speaking unnecessarily in prayer.

Key Terms
ندب (nadb): A recommended but non-obligatory act; its omission is not sinful but its performance is praiseworthy.
تسليمة (taslima): The salutation at the end of prayer, saying 'al-salamu alaykum wa rahmatullah' to the right and left.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Before starting the prayer.
B) After the first taslim.
C) After the two taslims (completing his own prayer).
D) It is not recommended at all.

Key Takeaway
The traveling imam's announcement is a sunnah act; the mufta bihi timing is after the two taslims, and it is not obligatory because the congregation can learn his status through other means.

Ruling on a Traveler Following a Resident and Vice Versa in Prayer
States validity conditions for musafir and muqim following each other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the validity conditions for a traveler (musafir) and a resident (muqim) following each other in prayer and apply the ruling to after-time scenarios.

Core Idea
A traveler can lead a resident anytime; a resident can lead a traveler only before time ends.

This lesson explores the mutual following (iqtida') between a traveler and a resident in prayer. The Hanafi school distinguishes between following within the prayer time and after it, based on the strength of each person's obligation. Understanding this ruling prevents common errors in congregational prayer when traveling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two scenarios: (a) a traveler (musafir) following a resident (muqim), and (b) a resident following a traveler. Determine the ruling for each case both within the prayer time and after the time has exited.
The passage explicitly states that the opposite (resident following traveler) is valid in both time and after time.
2
Examine the reasoning for traveler following resident after time
The text explains why it is invalid after time: the traveler's obligation (2 rak'at) does not change after the time exits. Following a resident (4 rak'at) creates a mismatch that cannot be reconciled after the time has passed.
The commentary elaborates on differences in the first sitting, recitation in the last two rak'at, and the opening takbir.
3
Analyze the dalil for resident following traveler
The hadith of the Prophet leading the people of Makkah while traveling and commanding them to complete their prayer is cited. Also qiyas: the traveler's prayer is stronger because the first sitting is obligatory for the traveler but supererogatory for the resident, and the weaker may be built on the stronger.
The dalil is a hadith from Abu Dawood and Malik; its transmission is zanni, but it is sufficient for this ruling.
4
Determine practical consequences after the imam's salam
After a traveler imam says salam, the resident followers complete their prayer individually, like a latecomer (masbuq). However, they do not perform sajdah al-sahw if they make an error while completing, and they do not follow another imam.
This is based on the principle that they are now praying on their own, not behind an imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعده لا يصح، وبعكسه صح فيهما. للمتابعة وقد زالت بخلاف ما لو اقتدى به بنية النفل ثم أفسد، حيث يلزمه الأربع؛ لأنه بالشروع التزم صلاة الإمام قصداً، وفي مسألتنا إنما قصد إسقاط الفرض عن ذمته، والتغير الحكمي قد زال بالإفساد، فيصلي ما كان عليه قبل الاقتداء ركعتين من التبيين»، و « مجمع الروايات»، و « البحر»، و«فتــح القدير»، (وبعده) أي: بعد خروج الوقت ( لا يصحُ) اقتداء المسافر بالمقيم فيما يتغير بالسفر وهـو الرباعية، وهذا تصريح بما علم التزاماً من السابق، لأن فرضه لا يتغير بعد الوقت لانقضاء السبب، كما لا يتغير بنية الإقامة، فيستلزم اقتداؤه بناء الفرض على غير الفرض حكماً، أما في القعدة إن اقتدى به في الشفع الأول، إذ القعدة فرض عليه لا على الإمام، أو في حق القراءة إن اقتدى بـه في الشفع الثاني، فإن القراءة فيه نفل على الإمام فرض على المقتدي، ولو لم يكن قرأ في الأوليين؛ لأن القراءة في الأخريين تلتحق بمحلها، فيبقى الثاني خالياً عن القراءة حكماً، أو في حق التحريمة؛ لأن إحرام الإمام انعقد على وجه لا يفترض عليه القعدة الأولى، ولا القراءة في الأخريين، ولا كذلك المؤتم المسافر، فيكون اقتداء المفترض بغير المفترض في حق مقتضى التحريمة، (وبعكسه) بأن اقتدی مقیم بمسافر (صح) الاقتداء (فيهما)، أي: في الوقت وفيما بعد خروجه، أمــا جــوازه في الوقـت فلأنَّه عليه الصلاة والسلام صَلَّى بِأَهْلِ مَكَّةَ وَهُوَ مُسَافِرُ فَقَالَ: أَتِمُّوا صَلَاتَكُمْ فَإِنَّا قُوْمِ سَفْر ، ولأن صلاة المسافر أقوى؛ لأن القعدة الأولى فرض في حقه نفل في حق المقيم، وبناء الضعيف عـلـى الـقـوي جائز، وأما بعد خروج الوقت، فلما ذكرنا من أن صلاته أقوى من صلاته، ثم إذا سَلَّم أتم المقيمون صلاتهم منفردين، لأنهم التزموا الموافقة في الركعتين، فينفردون في الباقي كالمسبوق، إلا أنهم لا سجود سهو عليهم إذا سهو، ولا يقتدي أحدهـم بـالآخر، كذا في « الخانية»
And after it (the time) it is not valid, and the opposite is valid in both. For following and it has ceased, contrary to if he followed him with the intention of supererogatory then nullified, where the four becomes incumbent upon him... (After it) meaning after the time exits, it is not valid for a traveler to follow a resident in what changes with travel, which is the four-rak'ah prayer... (And its opposite) meaning a resident follows a traveler, the following is valid in both (in time and after time)... As for its permissibility in time, because the Prophet ﷺ prayed with the people of Makkah while he was a traveler and said: 'Complete your prayer, for we are a traveling people'... and because the prayer of a traveler is stronger... then when he says the salam, the residents complete their prayer individually... they are like a latecomer, except that they do not have to perform prostration of forgetfulness if they err, and one does not follow the other.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings on following in prayer between a traveler (musafir) and a resident (muqim). First, a traveler following a resident after the prayer time has exited is invalid because the traveler's obligation is two rak'at and the resident's is four, and after time the traveler's obligation does not change. The commentary explains various aspects where the follower's duty differs from the imam's (e.g., the first sitting, recitation in the last two rak'at, and the opening takbir). Second, a resident following a traveler is valid both in and after time, based on the hadith of the Prophet ﷺ leading the people of Makkah while traveling, and also by analogy: the traveler's prayer is stronger in some respects (e.g., the first sitting is obligatory for the traveler but supererogatory for the resident), and the weaker may be built on the stronger. After the traveler says the salam, the residents complete their prayer individually like a latecomer, but with no prostration of forgetfulness for errors.

Key Terms
اقتداء (iqtidā'): The act of a follower (muqtadi) praying behind an imam with the intention of following him.
المسافر (al-musāfir): A traveler, one who undertakes a journey of three days (approx. 48 miles) or more, and for whom prayer is shortened to two rak'at.
المقيم (al-muqīm): A resident, one who is not traveling, and prays the full four rak'at.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid and he prays two rak'at
B) Valid and he must complete four rak'at
C) Invalid, he must repeat the prayer
D) Valid only if he intended to follow from the beginning

Key Takeaway
A traveler can follow a resident only before the prayer time ends; a resident can follow a traveler anytime, completing his prayer after the traveler's salam like a latecomer without sajdah al-sahw.

Recommendation for the Traveling Imam to Inform the Congregation
States the recommendation for the imam to tell the congregation he is a traveler

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the traveling imam informing his congregation and identify the preferred timing and legal classification (mandub).

Core Idea
The traveling imam should recommend the congregation complete their prayer, but it is not obligatory.

This passage addresses an important etiquette of prayer leadership: the traveling imam should inform resident followers that he is shortening the prayer. This prevents confusion and allows followers to complete their prayer correctly. While recommended, it is not a requirement, and the imam should ideally say it before beginning the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling classification
The act is the imam saying 'Atimmū ṣalātakum' to the congregation. Its ruling is mandub (recommended), not wajib or sunnah mu'akkadah.
The Arabic verb 'nudiba' is the key indicator.
2
Determine the preferred timing
The text states it is appropriate to say this before starting the prayer, to remove confusion from the beginning.
This timing is preferred but not strictly required; saying it after prayer is also acceptable.
3
Recognize the legal consequence of omission
Leaving it is not sinful, but the reward is lost. The prayer remains valid.
This distinguishes mandub from wajib.
4
Understand the anecdote's lesson
The story of Abu Yusuf shows that if the congregation already knows, the imam may not need to speak; also, speaking in prayer unnecessarily is disliked.
The anecdote reinforces that the announcement is a recommendation, not a requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونُدبَ للإمام أن يقول : أَتِمُّوا صلاتَكُمْ فَإِنِّي مُسافر. وينبغي أن يقول ذلك قبل شُروعِهِ في الصَّلاةِ؛ لدفع الاشتباه ابتداء. لطيفة: روي أن أبا يوسف لما حج مع هارون رحمهما الله وصلى بالناس ركعتين بمكة، ثم قال: أتموا يا أهل مكة صلاتكم، فإنا قوم سفر ، فقال له واحد منهم: نحن أعلم بهذا منك، فقال أبـو يوسف: لو علمت ما تكلمت في الصلاة، فقال هارون الرشيد: لو كان مثـل هـذا الـجـواب بـدلاً عــن الملك الذي أعطانيه الله تعالى لكنت أُسر بذلك كذا في مجمع الروايات».
And it is recommended for the imam to say: 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler.' And it is appropriate for him to say that before he begins the prayer, to remove confusion from the beginning. A pleasant narrative: It is narrated that when Abu Yusuf performed Hajj with Harun (may Allah have mercy on them) and led the people in two rak'at in Makkah, then said: 'Complete your prayer, O people of Makkah, for we are a traveling people,' one of them said: 'We know that better than you.' Abu Yusuf said: 'If I had known, I would not have spoken in the prayer.' Harun al-Rashid said: 'If there were a like of this answer in exchange for the kingdom that Allah has given me, I would be delighted.' So in Majma' al-Riwayat.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage contains a recommended (mandub) act for an imam who is traveling: after the final salam (or after the first salam, according to one view), he should inform the congregation, 'Complete your prayer, for I am a traveler.' This allows the residents to know they must complete the remaining two rak'at. It is better to say this before beginning the prayer to avoid confusion from the start. The story of Abu Yusuf illustrates that if a person is knowledgeable and the people are aware, it may be unnecessary; but the principle remains that the imam should inform. The anecdote also highlights the importance of not speaking unnecessarily in prayer, as Abu Yusuf regretted speaking even to inform.

Key Terms
ندب (nudiba): It is recommended (mandub), an action that is encouraged but not obligatory.
أتموا صلاتكم (atimmū ṣalātakum): The phrase used by the traveling imam to instruct the resident followers to complete their prayer to four rak'at after he finishes at two.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when is it recommended for the traveling imam to inform the congregation?
A) After the prayer entirely
B) Before starting the prayer
C) During the first rak'ah
D) Only if someone asks

Key Takeaway
The traveling imam should recommend residents complete their prayer before he begins, but it is a recommended courtesy, not an obligation.

Resident Follower's Recitation When Completing After Traveler Imam
Ruling that the resident does not recite when completing after traveler imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a resident follower recites al-Fatihah when completing prayer after a traveler imam, and explain the reason for this ruling.

Core Idea
A resident follower does not recite when completing after a traveler imam because recitation was already fulfilled with the imam.

When a traveler (musafir) leads a resident (muqim) in a four-rak'ah prayer, the traveler prays only two rak'at and then says salam. The resident must then stand and complete the remaining two rak'at. A key question arises: must the resident recite al-Fatihah in those two rak'at? This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling on this matter, based on the principle that the follower's recitation obligation is satisfied during the imam's prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the situation: a resident (muqim) prays behind a traveler (musafir) imam in a four-rak'ah prayer. The imam prays two rak'at and finishes. The resident stands to complete the remaining two rak'at.
This only applies to four-rak'ah prayers, not Maghrib or Fajr.
2
State the ruling
The text says: 'the resident does not recite in what he completes after his traveling imam finishes, according to the most correct view.' So the ruling is that he does NOT recite al-Fatihah in those two rak'at.
The phrase 'fi al-ashahh' indicates this is the relied-upon opinion.
3
Understand the rationale
The recitation obligation (wajib) was already fulfilled during the first two rak'at when the follower listened to the imam's recitation. Therefore, no recitation is required in the completing rak'at.
This differs from a latecomer (masbuq) who missed rak'at with the imam and must recite when completing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقرأ المقيم فيما يُتِمُّهُ بعد فراغ إمامِهِ المُسافر، في الأصح.
And the resident does not recite in what he completes after his traveling imam finishes, according to the most correct view.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This is a ruling specific to the scenario where a resident (muqim) follows a traveler (musafir) imam. After the imam says the salam at two rak'at, the resident stands to complete the remaining two rak'at. The question arises whether he must recite al-Fatihah in those two rak'at. The relied-upon opinion (al-ashahh) is that he does not recite, because he already performed the obligatory recitation with the imam during the first two rak'at. This differs from the case of a latecomer (masbuq) who missed the recitation with the imam and must recite when completing.

Key Terms
الأصح (al-aṣaḥḥ): The most correct view, the relied-upon position among the Hanafi scholars when there are different opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A resident follows a traveler imam in a four-rak'ah prayer. After the imam finishes two rak'at, the resident completes the remaining two. According to the Hanafi school, does the resident recite al-Fatihah in those two rak'at?
A) Yes, aloud
B) Yes, silently
C) No
D) It depends on the resident's intention

Key Takeaway
A resident follower does not recite al-Fatihah when completing prayer after a traveler imam, because the obligation was fulfilled by listening to the imam.

Qada of Missed Prayers Based on End of Time
Ruling with proof on qada of missed prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers the end of the prayer time for determining the number of rak'at in qada, and apply this rule to scenarios involving travel and residence.

Core Idea
The make-up prayer is performed in the form that was obligatory at the end of the original time.

This lesson addresses a common question: if a traveler misses a prayer, does he make it up as two rak'at or four? The Hanafi school gives a precise answer based on the end of the prayer time. We will examine the ruling, its proof, and the rationale behind it, including a comparison with the case of illness.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the immediate ruling: the missed prayer of travel is made up as two rak'at, and of residence as four. Notice the 'laf wa nashr' (coordinated wordplay) in the text.
2
Identify the Proof
The text gives the proof: 'because making up follows the manner of performance' (al-qaḍā' bi-ḥasab al-adā'). This is a general principle, but it is qualified by the consideration of the last part of the time.
3
Understand the Consideration of End of Time
The text explicitly states: 'the consideration is the last part of the time.' This means the traveler/resident status at the end of the prayer time determines the number of rak'at in qada, not the beginning.
Analogous to how eligibility (puberty, Islam, etc.) at the end of time makes the prayer obligatory.
4
Contrast with Illness Case
The text contrasts this with the case of illness: if one missed a prayer due to illness and could only gesture, he makes it up in health with full bowing and prostration. This is because the inability was not permanent and he has now regained ability.
In travel, the status (traveler/resident) is considered stable; it does not change in qada.
5
Apply to Scenarios
Given a scenario, determine the person's status at the end of the prayer time. If he is a traveler then, he makes up two rak'at; if a resident, four.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفائتةُ السّفرِ و) فائتة (الحَضَرِ تُقضى ركعتين، وأربعاً) فيه لف ونشر مرتب) أي: فائتة السفر تقضى ركعتين وفائتة الحضر أربعاً لأن القضاء بحسب الأداء، بخلاف ما لو فاته في المرض وكان لا يقدر إلا بالإيماء، حيث يقضيها في الصحـة راكعاً ساجداً قائماً، ولو كانت في الصحة يقضيها بالإيماء، لأن الركوع والسجود يسقطان بالعجز، فإذا قدر أتى بهما، وفيمـا نـحـن فـيـه لا يتغير بعد الاستقرار، والمعتبر فيه) أي: في لزوم الأربـع بالحضر، والركعتين بالسفر (آخرُ الوقتِ) فإن كان في آخره مسافراً صلى ركعتين، وإن كان مقيماً صلى أربعاً؛ لأنه المعتبر في السببية عند عدم الأداء فيما قبله من الوقت، ولهذا تلزمه الصلاة لو صار أهــلاً لها في آخر الوقت، ببلوغ، وإسلام، وإفاقة من جنون، وإغماء، وطهر من حيض، ونفاس، وتسقط بفقد الأهلية فيه بجنون، وإغماء ممتد، ونفاس ، وحيض.
And the missed prayer of travel and the missed prayer of residence are made up as two rak'at and four rak'at respectively — it is a coordinated wordplay. Meaning: the missed prayer of travel is made up as two rak'at and the missed prayer of residence as four, because making up follows the manner of performance. Contrary to if he missed it during illness and could only pray with gestures, then he makes it up in health bowing and prostrating while standing, even if it was in health he would make it up with gestures, because bowing and prostration are dropped due to inability, so when he is able he performs them. In our case, it does not change after stability. And the consideration in it, i.e., in the obligation of four in residence and two in travel, is the last part of the time: if at its end he is a traveler, he prays two; if a resident, he prays four, because it is the consideration in causation when not performing in the earlier part of the time. For this reason, prayer becomes obligatory if he becomes eligible at the end of time due to puberty, Islam, recovery from insanity, fainting, purity from menses and postpartum, and falls due to loss of eligibility due to insanity, prolonged fainting, postpartum, and menses.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage addresses the qada (make-up) of missed prayers. The key principle is that the prayer is made up in the form that was obligatory at the time it became due, specifically considering the end of the prayer time. If a person missed a four-rak'ah prayer while resident, he makes up four rak'at; if he missed a two-rak'ah prayer while traveling, he makes up two. The decisive factor is the status (traveler or resident) at the end of the time, not the beginning. This is because the obligation is determined by the end of the time, similar to how eligibility (like puberty or sanity) at the end of time makes the prayer obligatory. The passage contrasts with the case of illness, where inability at the time of performance affects the manner of making up—if one missed a prayer due to illness, he makes it up in full when recovered, because his ability has returned. But in our case, the status of travel or residence is stable and does not change in qada.

Key Terms
القضاء بحسب الأداء (al-qaḍā' bi-ḥasab al-adā'): The principle that a missed act of worship is made up in the same manner it was originally performed, with the same conditions and characteristics.
آخر الوقت (ākhir al-waqt): The last part of the prayer time; used to determine whether the prayer is considered traveled or resident for qada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person is traveling throughout the time of Zuhr, but becomes a resident (returns home) just before the time ends. How many rak'at should he make up if he missed the Zuhr prayer?
A) Two rak'at (traveler's prayer)
B) Four rak'at (resident's prayer)
C) He does not need to make it up
D) He prays two, then two separately

Key Takeaway
For missed prayers, the number of rak'at in qada is determined by the traveler/resident status at the end of the original prayer time, not the beginning.

Definitions and Nullification of Watan Asli and Watan Iqama
Defines two types of home and states what nullifies each

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of watan asli and watan iqama, describe what nullifies each, and apply these rules to determine a traveler's prayer status.

Core Idea
An original home is only nullified by another original home; a residence home is nullified by travel, another residence, or the original.

In Hanafi fiqh, a traveler's prayer obligations change based on where they are. Two key concepts—original home (watan asli) and residence home (watan iqama)—determine whether a traveler becomes a resident upon entering a place. This lesson defines each and explains what nullifies them, with a practical example.

Guided Path
1
Define Watan Asli
Identify the three ways a place becomes an original home: birth, marriage with settlement, or intention of permanent residence. Note that it is not nullified except by acquiring another original home.
2
Define Watan Iqama
Identify the condition for residence home: intention to stay fifteen days or more. Note that it is nullified by another watan iqama, by travel, or by returning to the original home.
The duration is mufta bihi fifteen days; some narrations say twenty.
3
Apply Nullification Rules
Use the example given: an Egyptian moves his family to Syria, making Syria his new watan asli. When he returns to Egypt as a traveler, his old watan asli is nullified, so entering Egypt does not make him a resident. He remains a traveler.
The ruling depends on whether the original home is still intact.
4
Determine Prayer Status
If a traveler enters a place that is his current watan asli, he becomes a resident and prays four rak'at. If he enters a place that was previously watan asli but is now nullified, he remains a traveler.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطل الوطن الأصلي بمثلِهِ فقط. ويبطُلُ وطن الإقامة بمثله، وبالسفر، وبالأصلي. والوطن الأصلي هو: الذي وُلِدَ فيه، أو تزوج، أو لم يتزوج، وقصدَ التَّعَيش، لا الارتحال عنه، ووطن الإقامة: موضع دوى الإقامة فيه نصف شهر فما فوقه. وفائدة هذا: أنه يتم الصلاة إذا دخله وهو مسافر قبل بطلانه. مثاله: مصري انتقل بأهله إلى الشام، فإذا عاد مسافراً ودخل [ مصره ] لم يتم بمجرد الدخول، فلو أبقى
And the original home is nullified only by its like. And the residence home is nullified by its like, by travel, and by the original home. The original home is: the place where one was born, or married, or if not married, intended to live permanently, not to leave. And the residence home: a place where one intends to reside for half a month or more. The benefit of this: that he completes the prayer if he enters it as a traveler before its nullification. Example: An Egyptian moved with his family to Syria; if he returns as a traveler and enters his (Egypt) he does not complete by mere entry; if he remains...
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage defines two types of 'home' (watan) in Hanafi fiqh, which affect the prayer of a traveler. 1) Watan Asli (original home): the place where one was born, or married (with intention to settle), or if unmarried, intends to live permanently without intending departure. This home is only nullified by acquiring another watan asli (e.g., moving permanently to another city). It is not nullified by a watan iqama or mere travel. 2) Watan Iqama (residence home): a place where one intends to reside for fifteen days or more. This home is nullified by: a) another watan iqama (i.e., intending residence elsewhere for 15+ days), b) setting out on a journey (travel), or c) returning to one's watan asli. The practical application: if a traveler enters his original home, he becomes a resident and must complete his prayer. But if he merely enters the city without the intent to stay, the ruling depends on whether his watan asli is still intact. The example: a person who has moved his family to Syria has made Syria his new watan asli; if he then returns to Egypt as a traveler, his entry does not make him a resident because his previous watan asli (Egypt) is nullified. The text seems to cut off with 'if he remains...' likely continuing the explanation.

Key Terms
الوطن الأصلي (al-waṭan al-aṣlī): Original home: the place of birth, marriage with settlement, or intention of permanent residence without intending to leave.
وطن الإقامة (waṭan al-iqāma): Residence home: a place where one intends to stay for fifteen days or more, without making it a permanent home.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He becomes a resident and prays four rak'at
B) He remains a traveler and prays two rak'at
C) He must pray two rak'at but can choose
D) His prayer is invalid until he intends to stay

Key Takeaway
An original home is only nullified by a new original home; a residence home is nullified by travel, another residence, or the original home, and these categories govern a traveler's prayer obligations.

Resident Follower's Recitation After Travelling Imam
Ruling on muqtadi muqim not reciting when completing prayer after imam musafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a resident follower's recitation when completing prayer after a travelling imam and explain the reason based on catching the beginning of the prayer.

Core Idea
A resident follower does not recite in his completion because the imam's recitation fulfilled the obligation.

When a resident prays behind a travelling imam, a unique situation arises: the imam prays two rak'ahs and gives salams, but the follower must complete four. Should the follower recite Quran in the extra rak'ahs? This lesson clarifies the relied-upon Hanafi position and the reasoning behind it, distinguishing this case from that of a latecomer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
Recognize the scenario: a resident (muqim) prays behind a travelling (musafir) imam. The imam completes two rak'ahs (qasr) and gives salams. The resident must continue to complete four rak'ahs.
This is a common case when a traveller leads residents in prayer.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the resident does not recite (qira'ah) in the remaining rak'ahs. The text says 'لا يقرأ' and labels this as the most correct (fi al-ashah).
Reciting would be makruh tahrimi according to some, but the prayer is still valid.
3
Understand the underlying reason (ta'lil)
The reason given is 'because he caught with the imam the beginning of his prayer, and the obligation of recitation has been fulfilled' (لأنه أدرك مع الإمام أول صلاته، وفرض القراءة قد تأدى).
Catching the first rak'ah means the imam's recitation covers the follower's obligation.
4
Distinguish from the latecomer (masbuq)
The ruling differs from the masbuq (one who joins late) who must recite in the missed rak'ahs because he did not catch the beginning of the prayer with the imam.
The masbuq missed the imam's recitation in those rak'ahs, so his obligation is not fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقرأ المؤتم المقيم فيما يُتِمُّهُ بعد فراغ إمامه المسافر في الأصح لأنه أدرك مع الإمام أول صلاته، وفرض القراءة قد تأدى، بخلاف المسبوق
And a resident follower does not recite in what he completes after the departure of his travelling imam in the most correct (opinion) because he caught with the imam the beginning of his prayer, and the obligation of recitation has been fulfilled, unlike the latecomer (masbuq).
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on a resident (muqim) who prays behind a travelling (musafir) imam. When the imam prays two rak'ahs (the traveller's prayer) and gives salams, the resident follower must complete his prayer to make it four rak'ahs. The ruling is that the resident follower does not recite (recite Quran) in the remaining rak'ahs that he completes on his own. This is because he caught the prayer with the imam from the very first rak'ah, so the obligation of recitation (which was fulfilled by the imam on his behalf) has already been discharged. This differs from the masbuq (one who joins late) who must recite because he missed part of the prayer with the imam. This is the relied-upon (asahh) position.

Key Terms
المؤتم (al-mu'tamm): The follower praying behind the imam.
المقيم (al-muqīm): A resident, one who is not travelling.
المسافر (al-musāfir): A traveller, one who undertakes a journey of at least three days (approx. 48 miles).
المسبوق (al-masbūq): One who joins the prayer late and misses one or more rak'ahs with the imam.
قراءة (qirā'ah): Recitation of the Quran in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must recite Fatihah in the remaining rak'ahs.
B) He does not recite because he joined the imam from the beginning.
C) He may recite if he wishes, but it is not required.
D) He must recite only if he missed the first rak'ah.

Key Takeaway
A resident follower does not recite in his completion after a travelling imam because catching the beginning of the prayer with the imam fulfills the obligation of recitation.

Invalidation of Watan Asli and Watan Iqama
Rules and examples on invalidation of watan types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for when a watan asli and watan iqama are invalidated, and apply these rules to determine whether a traveller completes or shortens prayer.

Core Idea
Watan asli is only invalidated by another watan asli; watan iqama is invalidated by travel, return, or another iqama.

The concept of domicile (watan) in Hanafi fiqh determines whether a traveller must shorten or complete the prayer. This passage from Nur al-Idah explains the two key types – watan asli and watan iqama – and the precise conditions under which each is invalidated. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the rulings on qasr and itmam in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of watan
Watan asli is the place where one was born, married, or intends permanent settlement. Watan iqama is a place where one intends to stay for 15 days or more.
Watan sukna (less than 15 days) is not recognized by the muhaqqiqun.
2
Understand invalidation of watan asli
Watan asli is only invalidated by its like – i.e., establishing another watan asli. It is not invalidated by watan iqama or travel, because a lesser thing cannot invalidate a greater.
This is based on the principle that a thing is only invalidated by its equal or superior.
3
Understand invalidation of watan iqama
Watan iqama is invalidated by its like (another watan iqama), by undertaking travel, or by returning to the watan asli.
Travel here means leaving the place with intention to go on a journey (not mere exit).
4
Apply the rule to the examples
The passage gives two examples: 1) An Egyptian who moves to Syria with family then returns to Egypt as a traveller. 2) A traveller who intends 15 days at a hostel and later goes to Bilbays. Trace how the watan status changes.
In the first example, if he marries in Syria without moving family, both remain watan asli and he completes in both.
5
Recognize the practical effect
Entering a watan asli or watan iqama while travelling makes one a resident for prayer purposes, so he must complete (itmam) his prayers.
If the watan iqama is invalidated before arrival, he remains a traveller and shortens (qasr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطل الوطن الأصلي بمثله فقط أي: لا يبطل بوطن الإقامة، ولا بالسفر؛ لأن الشيء لا يبطل بما دونه، بل بما هو مثله، أو فوقه، ولا يشترط تقدم السفر لثبوت الوطن الأصلي إجماعاً، ولا لوطن الإقامة في ظاهر الرواية، وإذا لم ينقل أهله بل استحدث أهلاً أيضاً ببلدة أخرى، فلا يبطل وطنه الأول، وكل منهما وطن أصلي له، ويبطل وطن الإقامة: بمثله، ويبطل أيضاً بإنشاء السفر بعده وبالعود للوطن الأصلي كما ذكرناه. والوطن الأصلي : هو الذي وُلِدَ فيه الإنسان، أو تزوّج فيه، أو لم يتزوج ولم يولد فيه ولكن قصد التعيش، لا الارتحال عنه. ووطن الإقامة: موضع صالح لها على ما قدمناه، وقد نوى الإقامة فيه نصف شهر فما فوقه. وفائدة هذا: أنه يتم الصلاة إذا دخله وهو مسافر قبل بطلانه. مثاله: مصري انتقل بأهله إلى الشام، فإذا عاد مسافراً ودخل مصر لم يتم بمجرد الدخول، فلو أبقى أهله وتزوج بالشام أيضاً، يتم بدخوله في كل من الوطنين، وإذا خرج يريد الشام فنوى الإقامة بالخانقاه السريا قوسية مثلاً، خمسة عشر يوماً، لم يبطل وطنه الأصلي، فإذا رجع إليه لحاجة يتم الصلاة فيه، فإذا خرج ودخل الخانقاه يقصر؛ لبطلان وطن الإقامة بها بالأصلي، وكذا لو خرج من الخانقاه بعد نية الإقامة فيها خمسة عشر يوماً، ولم يرجع إلى وطنه الأصلي، ولم ينو السفر حتى وصل إلى بلبيس مثلاً، فنوى الإقامة فيها خمسة عشر يوماً بطل وطن الإقامة بالخانقاه، وكذا إذا خرج منها ونوى السفر، حتى لو عاد إلى حاجة فيها يقصر، كما لو دخلها مسافراً بعد ذلك.
And the original domicile (watan asli) is only invalidated by its like, i.e., it is not invalidated by a domicile of residence (watan iqama) nor by travel, because a thing is not invalidated by what is lesser than it, but only by its like or above. The precedence of travel is not required for the establishment of watan asli by consensus, nor for watan iqama in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). If one does not move his family to another town but acquires a new family there, his first watan is not invalidated, and each of the two is an original domicile for him. And watan iqama is invalidated by its like, and also by undertaking travel after it, and by returning to the original domicile. Watan asli is where a person is born, or marries (even if not born there), or if neither born nor married but intends to settle and not leave. Watan iqama is a place where one intends to stay for half a month or more. The benefit of this is that one completes the prayer when entering it while a traveller before the invalidation of his travel status. Example: An Egyptian who moves with his family to Syria, then returns as a traveller and enters Egypt, he does not complete prayer merely by entering. If he keeps his family and also marries in Syria, then upon entering either of the two domiciles he completes. If he leaves for Syria and intends to stay at a hostel for fifteen days, his original domicile is not invalidated, so if he returns to it for a need, he completes prayer there. Then if he leaves and enters the hostel, he shortens because the watan iqama there is invalidated by the original domicile. Similarly, if he leaves the hostel after intending fifteen days, and does not return to his original domicile, and does not intend travel until he arrives at Bilbays, and intends fifteen days there, then the watan iqama at the hostel is invalidated. And if he leaves from there and intends travel, so that if he returns to the hostel for a need, he shortens, as if he enters it as a traveller after that.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage covers the rules of domicile (watan) which affect the traveller's prayer. There are three types: watan asli (original domicile), watan iqama (domicile of residence), and watan sukna (domicile of dwelling) which is not recognized. Watan asli is only invalidated by establishing another watan asli. Acquiring a watan iqama or traveling does not invalidate it. Watan iqama is invalidated by another watan iqama, by travel, or by returning to watan asli. Definitions: Watan asli is where one was born, married, or if neither, intends to settle permanently. Watan iqama is where one intends to stay for 15 days or more. The practical effect: if a traveller enters a place that is his watan asli or watan iqama, he becomes a muqim and must complete his prayer. The examples illustrate how multiple domiciles interact.

Key Terms
الوطن الأصلي (al-waṭan al-aṣlī): The original domicile where one was born, married, or intends permanent settlement.
وطن الإقامة (waṭan al-iqāma): A place where one intends to stay for at least 15 days.
وطن السكنى (waṭan al-suknā): A place where one intends to stay for less than 15 days.
قصر (qaṣr): Shortening the prayer from four to two rak'ahs for a traveller.
إتمام (itmām): Completing the full prayer (four rak'ahs) for a resident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person born in Makkah moves to Jeddah, marries there, and intends to settle. Later, he travels to Makkah for a visit. When he enters Makkah, should he complete or shorten his prayer?
A) He shortens because he is a traveller.
B) He completes because Makkah is still his watan asli.
C) He completes because Jeddah became his new watan asli, so Makkah is no longer watan asli.
D) He shortens because he never intended to stay in Jeddah.

Key Takeaway
Watan asli persists until another watan asli is established; watan iqama is temporary and easily invalidated.

Non-Consideration of Watan Sukna and Refutation of al-Zayla'i
Critique and refutation of watan sukna within Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the verified Hanafi scholars reject watan sukna and critique the example of Shaykh al-Zayla'i.

Core Idea
Watan sukna has no legal effect; travel overrides it entirely.

This lesson examines a dispute within the Hanafi school over the status of watan sukna — a place where a traveller intends to stay for less than 15 days. The verified scholars (muhaqqiqun) hold that it has no legal effect, while Shaykh al-Zayla'i attempted to give an example where it could matter. The passage refutes his example using stronger principles, affirming the relied-upon view that a traveller remains a traveller.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the issue is whether watan sukna (intent to stay <15 days) affects the traveller's prayer ruling.
2
State the muhaqqiqun position
The verified scholars do not consider watan sukna; they say there is no benefit because the traveller remains a traveller.
3
Examine al-Zayla'i's example
Al-Zayla'i presented a scenario: a man leaves his city for a village, intends <15 days, then leaves and later travels. When he passes through that village again, al-Zayla'i argued he should complete because no invalidation occurred.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author rejects the example because watan iqama (15+ days) is invalidated by travel, so watan sukna (weaker) is even more easily invalidated. Travel ends residence, so entering the village does not end travel.
The key principle: travel is the opposite of residence.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The muhaqqiqun view is relied upon; watan sukna is not recognized. The traveller shortens prayers even when entering a place of previous short stay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يعتبر المحققون وطن السكنى وهو ما ينوي الإقامة فيه دون نصف شهر وقد كان مسافراً قالوا: لأنه لا فائدة فيه؛ لأنه يبقى فيه على حاله مسافرا، وما ذكره الشيخ الزيلعي رحمه الله من تصوير فائدته بـ: رجل خرج من مصره إلى قرية لحاجة ولم يقصد السفر، ونوى أن يقيم فيها أقل من خمسة عشر يوماً يتم، ثم خرج منها غير مسافر، ثم بدا له أن يسافر قبل أن يدخل مصر، وقبل أن يقيم ليلة في موضع فسافر فإنه يقصر ، ولو مرّ بتلك القرية ودخلها؛ أتم لأنه لم يوجد ما يبطله مما هو فوقه أو مثله. انتهى. ممنوع لأن وطن الإقامة يبطل بالسفر، فكيف لا يبطل به وطن السكنى وهو دونه، وقد قال الشارح رحمه الله في تعليله بطلان وطن الإقامة بالسفر - لـ: أن السفر ضد الإقامة فلا تبقى معه. انتهى فيقصر إذا دخله؛ لعدم انتهاء سفره بدخوله والله أعلم.
And the verified scholars did not consider the domicile of dwelling (watan sukna), which is where one intends to stay for less than half a month while he was a traveller. They said: because there is no benefit in it; for he remains in it as a traveller. And what Shaykh al-Zayla'i mentioned by way of illustrating its benefit: 'A man left his original city to a village for a need and did not intend travel, and intended to stay there for less than fifteen days, so he completes. Then he left from it not as a traveller, then it occurred to him to travel before entering his city, and before staying a night anywhere, so he travelled, then he shortens. And if he passes that village and enters it, he completes because nothing that would invalidate his residence, above or like, occurred.' End of quote. It is rejected because watan iqama is invalidated by travel, so how does it not invalidate watan sukna which is lesser than it? And the commentator said in his argument for invalidation of watan iqama by travel: 'Travel is the opposite of residence, so residence does not remain with it.' So he shortens if he enters it, because his travel does not end by entering it. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage addresses the status of watan sukna (domicile of dwelling) in the Hanafi school. The verified scholars (muhaqqiqun) do not consider watan sukna as a legal concept because it has no practical effect: a traveller who intends to stay for less than 15 days remains a traveller, and no new ruling applies. The passage critiques an example given by Shaykh al-Zayla'i, where he tried to show a situation where watan sukna could have benefit. The critique argues that even in that example, watan sukna should not change the ruling, because travel is the opposite of residence, and if watan iqama (15+ days) is invalidated by travel, then watan sukna (less than 15 days) certainly should be. Thus, the correct ruling is that entering a place of watan sukna does not end one's travel status; the traveller continues to shorten prayer.

Key Terms
المحققون (al-muḥaqqiqūn): The verified scholars, those who have thoroughly investigated the issues.
وطن السكنى (waṭan al-suknā): A place where one intends to stay for less than 15 days.
الزيلعي (al-Zayla'ī): Shaykh al-Zayla'i, a Hanafi scholar known for his work on fiqh.
ممنوع (mamnū'): Rejected, not accepted as valid argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the verified scholars (muhaqqiqun), what is the ruling on a traveller who enters a village where he intends to stay for 10 days?
A) He becomes a resident and must complete his prayer.
B) He remains a traveller and shortens his prayer.
C) He must complete because the village is his watan asli.
D) He may choose between shortening and completing.

Key Takeaway
Watan sukna is not recognized; a traveller's status is only changed by watan iqama (15+ days) or watan asli.

When a Sick Person May Pray Sitting
Defines illness and ruling on sitting in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a sick person may pray sitting in the Hanafi school and apply the hadith of Imran ibn Husayn to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
Standing is the default in prayer; sitting is only a dispensation when standing is impossible or severely harmful.

This passage from 'Imdad al-Fattah' explains the definition of illness and the precise circumstances that permit a sick person to pray sitting. The author clarifies that mere discomfort does not justify sitting; only true impossibility or severe harm (risk of increased illness or delayed recovery) allows the dispensation. The ruling is anchored in the well-known hadith of Imran ibn Husayn where the Prophet progressively allowed lower postures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (The sick person's prayer). What is the ruling? (Sitting is permitted when standing is impossible or severely harmful).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Imran ibn Husayn. Identify its source and transmission (Bukhari, Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? Note the progressive permission: standing -> sitting -> on side -> lying down. This shows that standing is the baseline obligation and lower postures are concessions when higher ones are impossible.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text records al-Halwani's view: if the person can stand with support (leaning on a staff or servant), standing is still obligatory. Some later scholars allowed leaning without obligation, but the correct view (al-sahih) is that standing with support is required.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that if the sick person can stand even with support, he must do so. If he cannot stand at all or if standing causes severe harm, sitting is valid. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة المريض
من باب إضافة الفعل إلى فاعله، أو إلى محله، وأنه سائغ كقوله: جرح زيد لا يندمل، قاله الشيخ بدر الدين رحمه الله، كذا في «المستصفى»، وتصور مفهوم المرض ضروري، إذ لا شك أن فهم المراد من لفظ المرض، أجلى من فهمه من قولنا : معنى يزول بحلوله في بدن الحي اعتدال الطبائع الأربع، بل ذلك يجري مجرى التعريف بالأخفى، وعرفه في «كشف الأسرار» بأنه: حالة للبدن خارجة عن المجرى الطبيعي، وفي «المصباح» : مرض الحيوان من باب تعب، والمرض: حالة خارجة عن الطبع ضارة بالفعل، ويعلم من هذا أن الآلام والأورام أعراض عن المرض، وقال ابن فارس: المرض كلما خرج به الإنسان عن حد الصحة من علة، أو نفاق، أو تقصير في أمر، ومرض لغة قليلة قال الأصمعي: قرأت على أبي عمرو بن العلاء في {قُلُوبِهِم مَرَضٌ} [البقرة: ١٠] فقال لي: مَرْضِ يا غلام أي: بالسكون، والفاعل من الأولى مريض وجمعه مرضى، ومن الثانية مارض قال: ليس بمهزول ولا مارض.
إذا تعذر على المريض كل القيام بأن لا يمكنه القيام أصلاً يتركه، ولو قدر عليه متكئاً على عصا أو خادم، قال الحلواني: الصحيح أنه يلزمه القيام متكئاً ولا يجزئه غير ذلك، كما في «التبيين»، و«الفتح»، وهذا التعذر الحقيقي ومثله الحكمي في الحكم أشار إليه بقوله: أو تعسر كل القيام بوجود ألم شديد يترك القيام، فإن لحقه نوع مشقة لم يجز ترك القيام به، كما في «الفتح»، و«قاضي خان»، و«التاتارخانية»، أو خاف أي: غلب على ظنه بتجربة سابقة، أو إخبار طبيب مسلم حاذق زيادة المرض، أو خاف بطأه أي: طول المرض به أي: بالقيام صلّى قاعداً، بركوع وسجود؛ لما روى الجماعة إلا مسلماً، عن عمران بن حصين، قال: كانت بي بواسير فسألت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن الصلاة فقال: «صل قائماً، فإن لم تستطع فقاعداً، فإن لم تستطع فعلى جنب»، زاد النسائي: «فإن لم تستطع فمستلقياً لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها».
(۱) حاذق أي له معرفة تامة في الطب فلا يجوز تقليد من له أدنى معرفة فيه. حاشية ابن عابدين (٤٢٢/٢).
(٢) أخرجه البخاري في تقصير الصلاة، باب: إذا لم يطق قاعداً صلى على جنب (۱۱۱۷)، وأبو داود في الصلاة، باب صلاة القاعد (٩٥٢).
(۳) ذكره الزيلعي في نصب الراية (۱۷٥/١) ، وابن حجر في تلخيص الحبير (٢٢٥/١)، وقالا : رواه النسائي.
Chapter of the Sick Person's Prayer. It belongs to the chapter of adding an action to its doer or to its place, and that it is permissible like his saying: Zaid's wound does not heal. Sheikh Badr al-Din said it, as in 'al-Mustasfa'. Comprehending the concept of illness is necessary, since there is no doubt that understanding the intended meaning of the word illness is clearer than understanding it from our saying: a meaning that when it enters the body of a living being removes the balance of the four natures. Rather that is akin to defining with something more obscure. He defined it in 'Kashf al-Asrar' as: a state of the body outside the natural course. In 'al-Misbah': the illness of an animal is from the category of tiredness, and al-marad: a state outside the nature harmful to the action. From this it is known that pains and tumors are symptoms of illness. Ibn Faris said: al-marad is everything by which a person goes beyond the limit of health, from a disease, or hypocrisy, or shortcoming in a matter. 'Marida' is a rare language. Al-Asma'i said: I recited to Abu Amr ibn al-Ala' in {فِي قُلُوبِهِم مَرَضٌ} [al-Baqarah: 10] and he said to me: 'Marid' O boy, i.e., with sukun. The doer from the first is mariḍ and its plural is marḍā, and from the second is māriḍ. He said: not emaciated nor māriḍ. If standing completely is impossible for the sick person, in that he cannot stand at all, he leaves it. Even if he is capable of it leaning on a staff or a servant, al-Halwani said: the correct view is that he must stand leaning and nothing else suffices him, as in 'al-Tabyin' and 'al-Fath'. This is true impossibility, and likewise legal impossibility in the ruling. He indicated this by his saying: 'or if it is difficult' all standing due to severe pain, he leaves standing. If a type of hardship befalls him, he is not permitted to leave standing by it, as in 'al-Fath', 'Qadi Khan', and 'al-Tatarkhaniyya'. Or if he fears, i.e., it becomes overwhelming his assumption by prior experience or the report of a skilled Muslim doctor, increased illness, or fears its delay, i.e., the length of illness due to standing, he prays sitting with bowing and prostration. Because the group except Muslim reported from Imran ibn Husayn: I had hemorrhoids and asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about prayer, and he said: 'Pray standing, and if you cannot then sitting, and if you cannot then on your side.' Al-Nasa'i added: 'If you cannot then lying on your back. Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' (1) Skilled: i.e., possessing complete knowledge of medicine, so it is not permissible to follow one who has the least knowledge of it. Hashiyah Ibn Abideen (2/422). (2) Recorded by Bukhari in Taqsir al-Salat, chapter: If one cannot pray sitting, pray on side (1117), and Abu Dawud in al-Salat, chapter: Prayer of the sitting person (952). (3) Mentioned by al-Zayla'i in Nasb al-Rayah (1/175) and Ibn Hajar in Talkhis al-Habir (1/225), and they said: al-Nasa'i recorded it.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This is an expansion probe on the chapter of the sick person's prayer. The author begins by explaining the linguistic and technical meaning of 'marad' (illness), noting that it is clearer than defining it as a disturbance of the four humors. He then states the ruling: when standing is completely impossible, or severely difficult with real pain, or if standing would increase illness or delay recovery, the sick person prays sitting with bowing and prostration. The proof is the hadith of Imran ibn Husayn where the Prophet (peace be upon him) progressively allows sitting, then on side, then lying down. The footnote clarifies that a 'skilled Muslim doctor' is one with complete medical knowledge. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Halwani holds that if one can stand with support (leaning on a staff or servant), he must do so. The relied-upon view is that he must stand if possible, even with support. The ruling applies to both true and legal impossibility.

Key Terms
المرض (al-maraḍ): A state of the body outside the natural course that is harmful to action, as defined in Kashf al-Asrar.
التعذر (al-ta‘adhdhur): True impossibility: the person cannot stand at all, even with support.
التعسر (al-ta‘assur): Severe difficulty: standing causes severe pain or risk of increased illness.
القعد (al-qu‘ūd): Sitting position for prayer, including any posture as long as it is without harm.
حاذق (ḥādhiq): Skilled, possessing complete knowledge of medicine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following does NOT permit a sick person to pray sitting?
A) Standing causes severe pain in the leg
B) The person is over 70 years old and feels tired
C) A skilled Muslim doctor says standing will delay recovery
D) The person cannot stand even with the help of a crutch

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a sick person may pray sitting only when standing is impossible or when standing would cause severe harm or delay recovery, based on the hadith of Imran ibn Husayn.

Sitting Posture and Partial Standing for the Sick
Expands on two rulings for the sick in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the sick person's sitting posture and duty to stand partially, and apply the principle of obedience according to capacity.

Core Idea
Sick may sit any way; must stand as much as they can — obligation based on ability.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies two important rulings for the sick in prayer. First, the sick person may sit in any comfortable posture without restriction. Second, if the person can stand even partially, they must do so. The underlying principle is that obligations are calibrated to one's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states that the sick may sit as they wish (any comfortable posture) and that they must stand as much as they can if partial standing is possible.
The first ruling is about posture, the second about standing.
2
Examine the evidence for the sitting ruling
The commentator cites a narration from Abu Hanifa that any sitting posture is permissible without dislike, and uses a fortiori reasoning: since illness excuses the pillars (ruku', sujud), it even more so excuses mere postures.
The principle is 'illness drops the pillars, so postures are even more excused'.
3
Examine the evidence for partial standing
The commentator cites Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani: if one can stand for even one verse or one takbir, they must stand that much; otherwise the prayer may be invalid. This is confirmed as the madhhab with no disagreement.
The basis is 'obedience according to capacity' (al-ta'a bi-hasab al-taqa).
4
Apply the rulings to a scenario
Consider a person who can only stand for the opening takbir. According to the ruling, they must stand for that takbir and then sit for the rest of the prayer. If they sit from the beginning without standing, their prayer may be invalid.
The same applies if they can stand for one verse: they must stand for it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقعد كيف شاء، في الأصح، وإلا قام بقدر ما يمكنه، وإن تعذر الركوع والسجود صلى قاعداً بالإيماء، وجعل إيماءه للسجود أخفض من إيمائه للركوع. فإن لم يخفضه عنه لا تصح. ولا يرفع لوجهه شيء يسجد عليه، (ويقعد كيف شاء)، أي: كيف تيسر له بغير ضرر، من تربع أو غيره (في الأصح)، لما روي عن أبي حنيفة أنه يجلس كيف شاء من غير كراهة، إن شاء محتبياً، وإن شاء متربعاً، وإن شاء على ركبتيه، كما في التشهد؛ لأن عذر المرض أسقط عنه الأركان، فلأن يسقط عنه الهيئات أولى، كما في البدائع، (وإلا) أي: إن لم يتعسر كل القيام، بأن قدر على بعضه، (قام بقدر ما يمكنه)؛ لما عن أبي جعفر الهندواني: لو قدر على بعض القيام ولو قدر آية أو تكبيرة يقوم ذلك القدر، وإذا عجز قعد، وإن لم يفعل ذلك خفت أن تفسد صلاته، هذا هو المذهب ولا يروي عن أصحابنا خلافه، كذا في «معراج الدراية» و«العناية»، و«الفتح»، و«التبيين»، وهو المذهب الصحيح، كما في «التاتارخانية» عن «الخلاصة» انتهى. لأن الطاعة بحسب الطاقة، كما في «العناية» و«الدرر».
And he sits as he wishes, in the most correct view. Otherwise, if all standing is not difficult, he stands as much as he can. If bowing and prostration are impossible, he prays sitting with gesture, and makes his gesture for prostration lower than his gesture for bowing. If he does not lower it, the prayer is not valid. And nothing is raised to his face to prostrate on. (And he sits as he wishes), i.e., as is easy for him without harm, whether cross-legged or otherwise (in the most correct view), because it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he sits as he wishes without dislike, if he wishes squatting, or cross-legged, or on his knees, as in the tashahhud; because the excuse of illness has dropped the pillars from him, so it is more fitting that the postures are dropped. As in al-Bada‘i. (Otherwise), i.e., if all standing is not difficult, meaning he is capable of some of it, (he stands as much as he can); because of what is from Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani: if he can do some standing, even if it be one verse or one takbir, he stands that much, and if he cannot, he sits; and if he does not do that, I fear his prayer is invalid. This is the madhhab, and no disagreement is narrated from our companions. So it is in 'Mi'raj al-Diraya', 'al-‘Inaya', 'al-Fath', 'al-Tabyin', and it is the correct madhhab, as in 'al-Tatarkhaniyya' from 'al-Khulasa'. End. Because obedience is according to capacity, as in 'al-‘Inaya' and 'al-Durar'.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage expands on two rulings: (1) the sick person's sitting posture is unrestricted – he may sit in any way that is comfortable and without harm, whether cross-legged, squatting, or on his knees, without any dislike, as reported from Abu Hanifa. The reason given is that since the excuse of illness has dropped the actual pillars (rukū‘ and sujūd), it is even more appropriate that the mere postures (hay‘āt) are dropped. (2) If the sick person can stand partially (i.e., not complete impossibility), he must stand as much as he can, even if it is just for one verse or one takbir. If he fails to do so, his prayer may be invalid. This is the established madhhab with no reported disagreement from the Hanafi imams. The principle is 'obedience according to capacity' (al-ṭā‘ah bi-ḥasab al-ṭāqah).

Key Terms
التربع (al-tarabbu‘): Sitting cross-legged, one of the permissible postures for the sick in prayer.
الاحتباء (al-iḥtibā’): Sitting with knees drawn up and arms around them, another permissible posture for the sick.
الهندواني (al-Hindawānī): Abu Ja‘far al-Hindawani, a Hanafi jurist whose opinion is cited for partial standing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) He should not pray until he can stand fully
D) He must pray lying down

Key Takeaway
The sick are excused from the usual postures but must fulfill obligations to the extent of their ability.

Imā' (Gesture) for Bow and Prostration in Sick Prayer
Expands the ruling on imā' with conditions and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules of imā' for a sick person, identify the conditions for its validity, and explain the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
When you cannot bow or prostrate, gesture with your head — and make your prostration gesture lower than your bowing gesture.

This passage from Sharḥ Nūr al-Īḍāḥ details the fiqh of a sick person who cannot physically bow (rukū‘) or prostrate (sujūd). It establishes that such a person must pray sitting and gesture with the head, and that the gesture for prostration must be distinctly lower than that for bowing. The text also clarifies the true nature of imā' (lowering the head) and prohibits raising an object to the face for prostration. Two hadiths are cited as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is imā' (gesture) for rukū‘ and sujūd when unable to perform them physically. The ruling is that it is wajib (obligatory) and the prayer must be performed sitting.
The text stresses that lying down (muḍṭaji‘) does not suffice.
2
List the conditions for valid imā'
The sick person must sit (even if leaning), gesture with the head, and make the gesture for sujūd lower than that for rukū‘. Equal gestures invalidate the prayer.
The condition of being seated is crucial; the text explicitly states lying down is insufficient.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on the reality of imā'
Abu Hanifa allowed mere movement (taḥrīk) of the head, while Ibn al-Faḍl required active lowering (ṭa’ṭa’a). The relied-upon view is that the reality of imā' is lowering the head.
The text quotes al-Khānī and then concludes with the definitive position.
4
Locate the proof (dalīl) from hadith
Two hadiths are cited: one from al-Ṭabarānī prohibiting raising an object to prostrate on and commanding imā', and another from Jābir instructing to make sujūd lower than rukū‘.
These aḥādīth provide the textual basis for the rulings.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
If a sick person bows and prostrates by nodding equally, the prayer is invalid (bāṭil) because the required distinction is lost. The person must repeat the prayer.
This follows from the statement: 'fa-in lam yakhfiḍhū ‘anhu ... lā taṣiḥḥ'.

Source Text

And if bowing and prostration are impossible while he is capable of sitting even if leaning, he prays sitting with gesture for bowing and prostration with his head, and it does not suffice him lying down, as in al-Jawhara from al-Nihaya. And he makes his gesture with his head for prostration lower than his gesture with his head for bowing. Similarly, if he is incapable of prostration but capable of bowing, he gestures with both, as in al-Bahr from al-Bada’i. The reality of imā' is lowering of the head. And it is narrated [that it is] mere movement of it, as al-Khānī said in al-Bahr and Sharh al-Maqdisī: a sick person unable to gesture moves his head; according to Abu Hanifa it is permissible. Ibn al-Fadl said it is not permissible because no action occurred. End. So the reality of imā' is lowering of the head. End. Their words. If he does not lower it, i.e., the gesture for prostration from the gesture for bowing, by making them equal, it is not valid, as in Sharh al-Maqdisī and al-Bahr from al-Walwālijiyya, due to loss of prostration in reality and legally while being able. And nothing is raised (passive) to his face, like a piece of wood or stone, to prostrate on, because of his statement: 'Whoever among you can prostrate, let him prostrate; and whoever cannot, let him not raise to his face anything to prostrate on, but in his bowing and prostration let him gesture with his head.' Reported by al-Tabarani in his Mu‘jam. And because of the statement of Jabir: The Prophet (peace be upon him) visited a sick person and saw him praying on a cushion, so he took it and threw it away; then he took a stick to pray on, and threw it away, and said: 'Pray on the ground if you can, otherwise gesture, and make your prostration lower than your bowing.' Reported by al-Bazzar in his Musnad. So it is in al-Burhan, and similarly in al-Fath. And it is said in al-Mujtabā: ...
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This is an expansion probe on the rules of imā' (gesture) for a sick person who cannot perform actual bowing and prostration. The passage covers: (1) The sick person prays sitting and gestures with his head for both rukū‘ and sujūd; lying down is not sufficient. (2) The gesture for sujūd must be lower than that for rukū‘; if equal, the prayer is invalid because the requirement of a lower position for sujūd is lost. (3) The true nature of imā' is lowering the head (ṭa’ṭa’a), though a narration allows mere movement; the relied-upon view is lowering. (4) It is prohibited to raise an object to the face to prostrate on, as it implies prostrating on something which is not allowed when one can gesture. Two hadiths are provided: one from Tabarani and the story of Jabir. The principle is that the sick should gesture with the head and differentiate between bowing and prostration gestures.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-īmā’): Gesturing with the head for bowing and prostration when unable to perform actual movements; its reality is lowering the head.
الطأطأة (al-ṭa’ṭa’a): Lowering the head; the actual required action for imā'.
الولوالجية (al-Walwālijiyya): A Hanafi legal work cited for the invalidity of equal gestures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated

D) The prayer becomes a nafl prayer

Key Takeaway
The sick person's prayer is valid only when the head gesture for prostration is distinctly lower than that for bowing, as established by hadith and Hanafi juristic consensus.

Sitting Posture and Partial Standing for the Sick in Salah
Explains sitting postures and partial standing obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick person who cannot stand at all, and explain the obligation to stand partially when able.

Core Idea
Illness excuses full standing but not partial ability; sit conveniently and stand as much as possible.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah expands on the matn of Nur al-Idah regarding the prayer of a sick person. It addresses two scenarios: total inability to stand and partial ability. The ruling is clear: when complete standing is impossible, the sick person may sit in any comfortable posture; if some standing is possible, that much must be performed. The proof rests on narrations from Abu Hanifa and Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani, and the principle of obedience according to ability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between total inability to stand and partial ability. Identify which ruling applies to each scenario.
The first part deals with 'yaq'udu kayfa shaa' (sits however he wishes); the second with 'in lam yata'assar kull al-qiyam' (if not all standing is difficult).
2
State the hukm for total inability
Confirm that any sitting posture (cross-legged, on knees, etc.) is permissible without dislike. The hukm level is mubah.
The text explicitly mentions no karaaha (dislike).
3
Locate the dalil for total inability
The proof is a narration from Abu Hanifa that he sits however he wishes, supported by the principle that illness drops the arkan (pillars), so dropping the hay'aat (postures) is even more fitting.
This is a qiyas awla (priority analogy).
4
State the hukm for partial ability
If the person can stand for even a small part (e.g., one verse or takbir), he must stand for that part. Not doing so risks invalidity of the prayer.
The text cites Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani and confirms this as the relied-upon madhhab position.
5
Identify the evidence for partial ability
The evidence is the statement of Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani and the principle 'al-ta'atu bihasab al-taqah' (obedience is according to ability).
The author cites multiple sources (Ma'raj al-Diraya, al-Inaya, etc.) confirming no disagreement among Hanafi companions.

Source Text

And he sits however he wishes according to the most correct opinion, otherwise he stands as much as he is able. (And he sits however he wishes) meaning: as is convenient for him without harm, whether cross-legged or otherwise (according to the most correct opinion) due to what is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he sits however he wishes without dislike, whether sitting on his buttocks, cross-legged, or on his knees, as in the tashahhud; because the excuse of illness drops the pillars from him, so it is more fitting that the postures be dropped, as in al-Bada'i'. (And unless) meaning: if not all of standing is difficult, rather he is able to some of it, (he stands as much as he is able) due to what is from Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani: if he is able to some standing even if only a verse or takbir, he stands that much, and when unable he sits, and if he does not do that, I fear his prayer would be invalid. This is the madhhab and no difference from our companions is narrated, as in Ma'raj al-Diraya, al-Inaya, al-Fath, al-Tabyin, and it is the correct madhhab, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Khulasa. End. Because obedience is according to ability, as in al-Inaya and al-Durar.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary expanding the matn of Nur al-Idah regarding the prayer of a sick person who cannot stand. The author first states the ruling for total inability to stand: the sick person sits however is convenient, without harm. This includes sitting cross-legged, on the knees, or in the 'ihtiba' posture (sitting on the buttocks with knees drawn up). The proof is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that there is no dislike in any sitting posture, because the excuse of illness has already excused the pillars (like standing), so the recommended postures are even more excused. The author then addresses partial ability to stand: if the person can stand for some part of the prayer, he must stand for that part. The evidence is from Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani, and the author confirms this is the relied-upon position of the madhhab, citing multiple sources. The principle is that obedience is according to ability.

Key Terms
هيئات (hay'aat): Recommended postures and manners in prayer, such as specific sitting positions.

Ima (Gesture) for Ruku and Sujud: Rules and Proof
Ruling on ima (head gestures) for ruku and sujud when physically unable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid ima (gesture) in place of ruku and sujud, distinguish between valid and invalid ima, and apply the Hanafi ruling on the prohibition of raising objects to the face.

Core Idea
Ima substitutes for ruku and sujud when unable; sujud gesture must be lower than ruku; no object may be raised to the face.

When a sick person cannot physically bow or prostrate, the prayer is not abandoned. The Hanafi school provides a structured substitute: gestures (ima) with the head. This lesson unpacks the exact requirements for valid ima, the prohibition of using objects, and an internal Hanafi dispute on what constitutes 'gesture.' Understanding these details ensures that the sick person's prayer is valid according to the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing ruku and sujud via gestures (ima) when unable to do so physically. The ruling is that ima becomes wajib (obligatory) as a substitute. Also note the condition that the gesture for sujud must be lower than that for ruku.
The text states that equality of gestures invalidates the prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof comes from two hadith: one from al-Tabarani and the story of Jabir narrated by al-Bazzar. Both commands gesture with the head and prohibit raising objects to prostrate on.
The key phrase: 'fa la yarfa' ila wajhihi shay'an' and 'yumi bira'sihi'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit command 'yumi bira'sihi' indicates that the head gesture replaces actual sujud. The prohibition of raising objects reinforces that the substitute must be a gesture, not an object.
The hadiths are ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in practice.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
An intra-Hanafi dispute exists: Abu Hanifa allows mere movement of the head as valid ima, while Ibn al-Fadl requires actual bowing (lowering). The text also emphasizes that the sujud gesture must be lower than ruku.
This difference affects validity: if a person only moves his head, the prayer is invalid according to Ibn al-Fadl.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text sides with requiring bowing by stating 'haqiqat al-ima ta'tat al-ra's' (the reality of ima is bowing the head). Thus the mufta bihi position is that mere movement is insufficient; some lowering is required.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
