(فإن لم يخفِضْهُ) أي: الإيماء للسجود (عَنْهُ) أي: الإيماء للركوع بأن جعلهما على حد سواء (لا تصح)، كما في شرح المقدسي » و « البحر » ، عن « الولوالجية» لفقد السجود حقيقة وحكماً مع القدرة ( ولا يُرْفَعُ) بالبناء للمجهول (الوجهه شيء) كخشبة وحجر (يسجد عليه)؛ لقوله : ( مَنِ اسْتَطَاعَ مِنْكُمْ أنْ يَسْجُدْ فَلْيَسْجُدْ، وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ، فَلا يَرْفَعُ إِلى وَجْهِهِ شَيْئًا يَسْجُدُ عَلَيْهِ، وَلَكِنْ فِي رُكُوعِهِ وَسُجُودِهِ يُوْمِي بِرَأْسِهِ )، رواه الطبراني في «معجمـه ولـقـول جابر : ( أنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عَادَ مَريضَاً، فَرَاهُ يُصَلِّي عَلَى وِسَادَةٍ، فَأَخَذَهَا فَرَمَى بِهَا، فَأَخَذَ عَودَاً ليصلِّي عَلَيْهِ فَرَمَى به، وقال: صلَّ عَلَى الأرض إنِ اسْتَطَعْت، وإلا فأوم إيماء، واجعل سجودك أخْفَضَ مِنَ رُكُوعِكِ ) () ، رواه البزار في مسنده» ، كذا في ( البرهان » ، ومثله في «الفتح»، وقال في « المجتبى» : فإن فعل وخفض رأسه، صح، وإلا لا.
(And if ruku and sujud become difficult) he prays sitting with gestures for ruku and sujud with his head, and it is not valid for him lying on his side, as in al-Jawhara from al-Nihaya. (And he makes his gesture) with his head (for sujud lower than his gesture) with his head (for ruku). Likewise if he is unable to prostrate but able to bow, he gestures for both, as in al-Bahr from al-Bada'i. The reality of ima is bowing the head, and it is narrated mere movement of it. When in al-Bahr and Sharh al-Maqdisi from al-Khani: a sick person unable to gesture moved his head; from Abu Hanifa it is permissible, and Ibn al-Fadl said it is not permissible because no action occurred. End. So the reality of ima is bowing the head. End. Their statement. (Then if he does not lower it) meaning the gesture for sujud (from it) meaning the gesture for ruku, making them equal, (it is not valid), as in Sharh al-Maqdisi and al-Bahr from al-Walwalijiyya, due to the loss of sujud in reality and in ruling despite ability. (And nothing is raised) passive (to his face such as a piece of wood or stone to prostrate on) due to his statement: 'Whoever among you can prostrate, let him prostrate, and whoever cannot, let him not raise anything to his face to prostrate on, but in his ruku and sujud let him gesture with his head', narrated by al-Tabarani in his Mu'jam; and due to the statement of Jabir: 'The Prophet visited a sick man and saw him praying on a cushion, so he took it and threw it away, and took a stick for him to pray on and threw it away, and said: Pray on the ground if you can, otherwise gesture, and make your sujud lower than your ruku', narrated by al-Bazzar in his Musnad. So in al-Burhan, and similar in al-Fath. And he said in al-Mujtaba: 'Then if he does so and lowers his head, it is valid, otherwise not.'
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a sick person cannot perform the actual bowing (ruku) and prostration (sujud) but can sit. The ruling is that he prays sitting and makes gestures (ima) with his head: a slight bow for ruku and a lower bow for sujud. The text clarifies that the gesture for sujud must be lower than that for ruku; making them equal invalidates the prayer. The reality of ima is defined as bowing the head; mere movement is disputed: Abu Hanifa allows it, Ibn al-Fadl does not. The text then addresses a key prohibition: the sick person must not raise anything (like a stone or wood) to his face to prostrate upon; rather, he should gesture. Two hadith proofs are cited: one from al-Tabarani and the story of Jabir. Finally, if the sick person does raise something and still lowers his head appropriately, his prayer is valid (though disliked) according to al-Mujtaba. The passage thus establishes the rules for ima when ruku and sujud are impossible.

Key Terms
إيماء (ima'): Gesture with the head replacing ruku and sujud when unable to perform them physically.
طأطأة الرأس (ta'tat al-ra's): Bowing or lowering the head, which is the actual practice of ima according to the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a sick person makes the gesture for sujud equal to the gesture for ruku?
A) The prayer is valid but disliked
B) The prayer is invalid
C) He must repeat the prayer
D) He must perform sajdah al-sahw

Key Takeaway
Ima (head gesture) validly replaces ruku and sujud when unable, provided the sujud gesture is lower than ruku and no object is raised to the face.

Inability to Sit: Gestures While Lying Down
Ruling on prayer gestures when unable to sit: lying on back or side with preference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer gestures for a person who cannot sit, explain the preferred lying positions, and identify the supporting hadith.

Core Idea
When sitting for prayer is impossible, gestures (ima) become wajib, and lying on the back is the most preferred position.

This lesson addresses the final stage of incapacity in prayer: when a person cannot sit at all, even with support. The Hanafi school prescribes gesturing (ima) while lying down, with a clear hierarchy of positions. We will explore the textual evidence and the reasoning behind preferring the back over the side.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act being ruled upon: prayer by gestures while lying down. The ruling is wajib when sitting is impossible.
This is a substitution (badal) for the normal sitting posture.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith 'فَإِنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِع فَعَلَى قَفَاهُ' (If he cannot, then on his back).
This is a hadith ahad, so its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the preference. The command 'on his back' indicates that lying on the back is the default and most complete way to face the qibla.
The text also uses reasoning: 'because facing the qibla is greater in it.'
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that other schools may have different views, but the Hanafi school relies on this hadith and the principle of optimal qibla orientation.
Within Hanafi, there is no disagreement; the ruling is standard Zahir al-Riwayah.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand why lying on the back is preferred over the side: the hadith explicitly mentions the back, and it allows better orientation to the qibla (the entire body faces the qibla).
Lying on the right side is preferred over the left, but the back is 'awla' (more preferable).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن تعسّرَ القُعود) فلم يقدر عليه متكئاً ولا مستنداً إلى حائط أو غـيـره بـلا ضرر أومأ، ولو قدر على القعود مستنداً فتركه لم تجز على المختار، فإذا تعسر الاستناد (أَوْماً)، كما لو قدر (مُستلقياً) على قفاه أو على جَنْبِهِ والأيمن أفضل من الأيسر، وبه ورد الأثر)، ( والأوّلُ) وهو الاستلقاء على قفاه (أَوْلَى) من الجنب الأيمن إن تيسر بلا مشقة، لحديث: «فَإِنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِع فَعَلَى قَفَاهُ (٣) ولأن التوجه للقبلة فيه أكثر، إذ إشارته إليها وهـو الـكـعـبـة قبلـة إلـى عـنـان السماء، والثاني: المضطجع إلى جانب قدميه، والاستلقاء محكم، والجنب وإن ورد به الحديث كما
(And if sitting becomes difficult) so he is unable to sit leaning or supporting against a wall or other without harm, he gestures. If he is able to sit with support but leaves it, it is not valid according to the chosen opinion. So when support becomes difficult (he gestures) as if he is able (lying on his back) on his nape or on his side, and the right side is better than the left, and the narration has come regarding that. (And the first) which is lying on his back (is more preferable) than the right side if it is possible without difficulty, due to the hadith: 'Then if he cannot, then on his back,' and because facing the qibla is greater in it, as his indication to it while the Ka'ba is the qibla towards the canopy of the sky. The second is lying on his side with the feet forward, and lying on the back is more firm, and the side although the hadith has come regarding it as well.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage deals with a further level of incapacity: when the sick person cannot even sit, even with support. The ruling is that he gestures (ima) while lying down. He may lie on his back (mustalqiyan) or on his side (janb). The text states that the right side is better than the left, but the preferable position is lying on the back, as supported by a hadith: 'If he cannot, then on his back.' The reasoning is that facing the qibla is better achieved lying on the back, as the entire body is oriented. Lying on the side is also valid, but the back is considered 'muhkam' (firmer) and more in line with the tradition. The passage emphasizes that if the person can sit with support (e.g., leaning on a wall), he must do so; leaving it invalidates the prayer on the chosen opinion.

Key Terms
مستلقياً (mustalqiyan): Lying on the back, face upward.
مضطجعاً (mudtaji'an): Lying on the side.
إيماء (ima): Gestures with the head to indicate ruku and sujud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the most preferable position for a sick person who cannot sit at all?
A) Lying on the left side
B) Lying on the right side
C) Lying on the back
D) Lying face down

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling provides a clear hierarchy: lying on the back is best, then right side, then left side, all valid with gestures.

Prayer Positions When Sitting Is Difficult: Lying on Back vs Side
Ruling with proof on sick prayer positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the preferred lying position for prayer when sitting is impossible, and identify the practical adjustments (pillow, knees) required for its validity.

Core Idea
Lying on the back is preferred over the side for sick prayer because it maximizes qibla orientation and follows the explicit hadith.

When illness prevents a person from sitting, Islamic law provides a merciful concession: prayer through gestures (ima') while lying down. But which lying position is best? The Hanafi school gives clear guidance, preferring the back over the side based on prophetic instruction and the principle of facing the qibla as much as possible. This lesson unfolds the ruling and the practical steps to perform it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation and the ruling
Recognize that when sitting is impossible even with support, a sick person may pray gesturing while lying. The prayer is valid (sahih) but following the preference is afdal.
The default for prayer is standing, then sitting, then lying with gesture. This lesson addresses the last stage.
2
Determine the preferred position
The passage states that lying on the back (mustalqiyan 'ala qafah) is أَوْلَى (preferred) over lying on the right side, even though a hadith mentions the side.
Right side is better than left, but back is better than both.
3
Understand the proof
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'If he cannot, then on his back.' Additionally, the back allows greater qibla orientation since the whole body faces the Kaaba and gestures are easier.
The hadith is narrated by al-Daraqutni and is zanni in transmission and meaning.
4
Harmonize the conflicting hadith about the side
The author explains that 'lying on the side' in some narrations may idiomatically mean 'prolonged illness' rather than literal posture, or it applied to a companion who could not lie on his back. The back is the authoritative (muhkam) position.
The choice is available but the preferred is the back.
5
Note practical etiquettes
When lying on the back, place a pillow under the head so the face points to the qibla, not the sky. Also, draw up the knees to avoid extending the legs towards the qibla, which is disliked.
These actions imitate the sitting posture and enable proper gesture for ruku' and sujud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وإن تعسّرَ القُعود) فلم يقدر عليه متكئاً ولا مستنداً إلى حائط أو غـيـره بـلا ضرر أومأ، ولو قدر على القعود مستنداً فتركه لم تجز على المختار، فإذا تعسر الاستناد (أَوْماً)، كما لو قدر (مُستلقياً) على قفاه أو على جَنْبِهِ والأيمن أفضل من الأيسر، وبه ورد الأثر)، ( والأوّلُ) وهو الاستلقاء على قفاه (أَوْلَى) من الجنب الأيمن إن تيسر بلا مشقة، لحديث: «فَإِنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِع فَعَلَى قَفَاهُ (۳) ولأن التوجه للقبلة فيه أكثر، إذ إشارته إليها وهـو الـكـعـبـة قبلـة إلـى عـنـان السماء، والثاني: المضطجع إلى جانب قدميه، والاستلقاء محكم، والجنب وإن ورد به الحديث كما رويته، قد يطلق ويراد به السقوط، يقال: بقي فلان شهراً على جنبه، إذا طال مرضه، وإن كان مستلقياً، وقيل: كان عمران يمنعه مرضه من الاستلقاء فلذلك أمر أن يصلي على الجنب، فالخيار لـه والأولى الاستلقاء، وقدمنا جواز التوجه لما قدر عليه، ويسقط التوجه إلى القبلة بعذر المرض ونحوه. (وَ) إذا صلى مستلقياً على ما هو الأولى (يَجْعلُ تحت رأسه وسادة)، أو نحوها (ليصير وجهه إلى القِبْلَةِ (لا) إلى (السَّماءِ ) ويكون شبه القاعد؛ ليتمكن من الإيماء بالركوع والسجود، إذ حقيقة الاستلقاء تمنع الأصحاء عن الإيماء بهما فكيف بالمرضى. (وينبغي) للمريض (نَصْبُ رُكْبَتَيْهِ إِنْ قَدَرَ حتَّى لا يَمُدَّهُما ) ، فيمتد برجليه ( إلى القبلة) وهو مكروه للقادر على الامتناع عنه، كما في «مجمع الروايات»، وغيره.
(And if sitting is difficult) so he cannot sit leaning or resting against a wall or other without harm, he gestures. And if he is able to sit with support but leaves it, it is not permissible according to the chosen view. When support becomes difficult, he gestures, as if able (lying on his back) or on his side, and the right side is better than the left, and the tradition has come with that. (And the first) i.e., lying on his back (is preferable) over the right side if it is easy without hardship, due to the hadith: 'If he cannot, then on his back' and because facing the qibla is greater in it, since his indication towards it and the Kaaba is the qibla towards the expanse of the sky. And the second: lying on his side with his feet, and lying on the back is authoritative, and the side even though the hadith comes as we narrated, it may be used and intended to mean falling ill, it is said: So-and-so remained on his side for a month, meaning his illness prolonged, even if he was lying on his back. And it was said: Imran's illness prevented him from lying on his back, so he was ordered to pray on his side. So he has a choice, and lying on the back is preferable. And we have preceded the permissibility of facing what he is able, and facing the qibla is dropped due to the excuse of illness and the like. (And) if he prays lying on his back as preferable, (he places under his head a pillow) or similar (so that his face faces the qibla, not the sky) and he becomes like one sitting, to enable gesture for bowing and prostration, since the reality of lying on the back prevents the healthy from gesturing with them, so how about the sick? (And it is recommended) for the sick (to erect his knees if he can, so that he does not extend them) stretching his legs (towards the qibla) and it is disliked for one able to refrain, as in Majma' al-Riwayat and others.

This passage is a ruling with proof on the positions for prayer when a sick person cannot sit. The ruling: if the person cannot sit even with support, he should gesture while lying (ima'). Lying on the back (mustalqiyan 'ala qafah) is preferred over the side, contrary to some narrations that preferred the right side. The author harmonizes the hadith by explaining that 'on his side' may idiomatically mean prolonged illness, not literal posture. He also provides practical guidance: when lying on the back, place a pillow under the head to face the qibla, and draw up the knees so that the legs do not point towards the qibla, which is disliked. The proofs include the hadith 'If he cannot, then on his back' (narrated by al-Daraqutni) and the principle of maximizing qibla orientation.

Key Terms
المستلقي (al-mustalqi): Lying on one's back with the face upward; the preferred position for prayer when unable to sit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When sitting is impossible, pray lying on the back; it is the preferred Hanafi position supported by hadith and the principle of maximizing qibla orientation.

Qada' When Ima' is Impossible for Extended Duration
Ruling with proof on qada' for extended inability to gesture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada' for a sick person unable to gesture for more than a day and night, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute and its resolution.

Core Idea
For extended inability to gesture, qada' is not required—the later Hanafi view prevails.

In the previous lesson, you learned about ima' (gesture) as a substitute for bowing and prostration. But what happens when even gesture becomes impossible for an extended period? This lesson tackles a nuanced question: must a person who cannot gesture for more than a day and night make up those prayers upon recovery? The answer reveals an important intra-Hanafi dispute, with the later scholars adopting a more lenient position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling's scope
The act in question is qada' (making up missed obligatory prayers) when a sick person is unable to perform even the minimal gesture (ima') for an extended period. The scope differentiates between short duration (less than a day and night) and long duration (exceeding a day and night).
2
Locate the agreed ruling
For inability lasting less than a day and night, qada' is obligatory by consensus. This is the starting point of the discussion.
This is the 'agreed' part mentioned in the text.
3
Identify the dispute
For inability exceeding a day and night, there are two views within the Hanafi school: (a) qada' is obligatory as long as the person understands the address (al-Hidayah, earlier), and (b) qada' is not required if the inability continues for more than five prayers (al-Tajnis).
Both views are from the same author, al-Marghinani, showing a refinement of his own position.
4
Trace the reasoning
The first view relies on the person's continued understanding of the address (khitab), which keeps the obligation intact. The second view analogizes (qiyas) to the case of a long-term unconscious person, for whom the khttab is effectively suspended. Ibn al-Humam clarifies that the reasoning for short duration obligation does not extend to long duration.
The proof is rational (dalil 'aqli) via qiyas.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Ibn al-Humam and later Hanafi scholars prefer the view from al-Tajnis: qada' is not wajib for prayers missed beyond a day and night (or five prayers). This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.
The tarjih authority is Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وإن تعذر الإيماء) برأسه (أُخْرَتْ عنه) الصلاة القليلة، وهـي: صلاة يوم وليلة فما دونها اتفاقاً، وأما إذا كثرت وزادت على صلاة يوم وليلة (ما دام يفهم) مضمون ( الخطاب)، فإنه يقضيها في رواية، قال في ( الهداية : هو الصَّحيحُ)، وتبعه صاحب « الـكـنـز فـيـه، وفي « المستصفى » : قال : الصحيح أنه لا يسقط القضاء لأنه يدرك مضمون الخطاب، فلا يكون نظير المغمى عليه. انتهى. وقال ( الكمال بن الهمام رحمه الله تعالى من تأمل تعليل الأصحاب في الأصول لإلزام القضاء، فيما دون يوم وليلة للذي جن أو أغمي عـلـيـه، لا فيما زاد عـلـى صـلاة يـوم وليلة، انقدح في ذهنه إيجاب القضاء على هذا المريض، الذي عجز عن الإيماء برأسه إلى يوم وليلة، حتى يلزمه الإيصاء به إن قدر عليه بطريق وسقوطه إن زاد انتهى. فهذا المحقق « ابن الهمام» يميل إلى القول بعدم الوجوب بمجرد فهم الخطاب، إذا زاد العجز على صلاة يوم وليلة، خصوصاً (و) قد (جزم صاحب ( الهداية » ) مخالفاً لها، ( في كتابه « التجنيس والمزيد » : بسقوط القضاء إذا دام عجزه عن الإيماء) برأسه أكثر من خمس صلوات، وإن كان يفهم مضمون (الخطاب)، فقد قال بخلاف قوله في « الهداية » ، ونص عبارته في ( التجنيس : المريض إذا صار بحال لا يستطيع الصلاة لا بالإيماء، ولا بغير الإيماء فمات، لا يجب عليه شيء من كفارات الصلوات، ولا يكون مؤاخذاً؛ لأنـه لـم يقدر على أداء الصلاة في حال الحياة ليجب الأداء، أو لا يجب خلفه وهو : الفدية، فإن بــرى مـن ذلـك وصح إن كان ما ترك من الصلوات أقل من يوم وليلة قضى تلك الصلوات؛ لأنه قدر على أداء الصلاة في زمان له خلف فلزمه، وإن كان أكثر من يوم وليلة لم يجب عليه قضاء تلك الصلوات؛ لأنه لم يصر خلفاً
(And if gesture is impossible) with his head, (the few prayers are delayed from him), namely the prayer of a day and night or less, by agreement. As for if they become many and exceed the prayer of a day and night, as long as he understands the content of the address, then he makes them up in one narration. He said in al-Hidayah: That is the correct one. And the author of al-Kanz followed him. And in al-Mustasfa: He said: The correct view is that qada' is not dropped because he understands the content of the address, so he is not like one who is unconscious. End. And al-Kamal ibn al-Humam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Whoever ponders the reasoning of the scholars in the principles for obligating qada' for less than a day and night for one who goes mad or is unconscious, not for what exceeds the prayer of a day and night, it becomes apparent in his mind the obligation of qada' on this sick person who is unable to gesture with his head for up to a day and night, so that it becomes necessary for him to bequeath it if he is able by way, and it falls if it increases. End. So this investigator Ibn al-Humam tends towards the view of non-obligation merely by understanding the address, if the inability exceeds the prayer of a day and night, especially since the author of al-Hidayah has stated contrary to it, in his book al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid: the dropping of qada' if his inability to gesture continues for more than five prayers, even if he understands the content of the address. So he said contrary to his statement in al-Hidayah. And the text of his statement in al-Tajnis: The sick person if he reaches a state where he cannot pray, neither by gesture nor otherwise, and dies, nothing of the expiations of prayers is due from him, nor is he held accountable, because he was not able to perform the prayer in his lifetime for performance to be obligatory, or that his substitute (i.e., the fidyah) is not obligatory. If he recovers from that and becomes healthy, if the prayers he missed were less than a day and night, he makes them up because he was able to perform the prayer in a time that has a substitute, so it becomes obligatory. And if they were more than a day and night, it is not obligatory for him to make up those prayers because it does not become a substitute.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on whether a sick person must make up (qada') prayers when he is unable to gesture (ima') for an extended period. The core issue: if the inability lasts less than a day and night, qada' is obligatory by agreement. The dispute occurs when it exceeds that. Some (like the author of al-Hidayah in his earlier work) hold that qada' is obligatory as long as the person understands the address (i.e., is conscious). However, al-Hidayah's author later in al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid ruled that if the inability continues for more than five prayers, qada' is not required. Ibn al-Humam supports this later view, distinguishing between temporary and prolonged incapacity. The proof is analogical: just as one who is unconscious for more than a day and night does not have to make up prayers, the sick person who cannot even gesture for that long is similar, because the obligation of prayer is conditional on ability. The passage thus shows an intra-Hanafi dispute resolved in favor of the more lenient view.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-ima'): Gesture with the head for bowing and prostration when physical movement is impossible.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up missed obligatory prayers after the ability returns.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what is the ruling on making up prayers missed due to inability to gesture for a duration of two days and nights?
A) All missed prayers must be made up.
B) Only the first day and night must be made up; the rest are not obligatory.
C) No prayers need to be made up at all.
D) The person should perform fidyah instead of qada'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that when inability to gesture for prayer exceeds a day and night, qada' is not obligatory, based on qiyas with the unconscious.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Qada for the Patient Unable to Gesture
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada for prolonged inability to gesture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on qada for a patient unable to gesture, identify the mufta bihi, and evaluate the evidence from al-Hidayah and later authorities.

Core Idea
If a patient cannot even gesture for over a day and night, the obligation to make up missed prayers is dropped.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: a sick person is so weak that they cannot even make the minimal gesture (imā') with their head during prayer. The key question is whether they must make up (qada) those missed prayers upon recovery. You will encounter two conflicting views within the Hanafi school: one requiring qada as long as comprehension remains, and the other excusing it for prolonged inability. We will navigate the evidence, the principle of hardship, and the preferred view for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Scenario and Initial Rulings
First, note the recommended posture for a sick person praying on their back: pillow under head to face qibla, knees bent. Then, the core issue: if head gesture is impossible, the prayer is postponed. Determine the duration: for a short period (less than a day and night) there is consensus that qada is required. Our focus is when inability extends beyond that.
The 'day and night' threshold matches the ruling for the unconscious person.
2
Identify the Two Intra-Hanafi Positions
There are two positions: (1) Qada is obligatory as long as the patient understands speech (attributed to al-Hidayah and al-Kanz). (2) Qada is not obligatory if the inability exceeds a day and night (or five prayers), even if the patient understands speech (supported by al-Tajnīs and many later scholars).
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf; both sides claim Hanafi authority.
3
Analyze the Evidence and Reasoning
Examine the hadith: 'Allah is most entitled to accept his excuse.' The opposing view interprets it as excusing delay; the dropping view interprets it as excusing the obligation entirely. Also, analogy with prolonged unconsciousness (where qada is waived) supports the dropping view.
The difference hangs on the interpretation of 'udhr (excuse).
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi (Ruled-upon Position)
The text states that the dropping view is ẓāhir al-riwāyah (the apparent transmission), chosen by Qāḍī Khān, al-Sarakhsī, and others, and that the fatwā is upon it. This is the operative ruling for practical application.
Later Hanafi scholarship (mutakhkhirūn) heavily favors the dropping view.

And he places a pillow under his head, so that his face faces the qiblah, not the sky. And it is recommended to erect his knees if he can, so that he does not extend them toward the qiblah. If gesturing (with the head) is not possible, the prayer is postponed as long as he understands speech. He said in al-Hidayah: That is the correct view. And the author of al-Hidayah in al-Tajnīs wa al-Mazīd explicitly stated that qada is dropped if his inability to gesture continues for more than five prayers, even if he understands speech. We have narrated it; it may be used to mean dropping. It is said: So-and-so remained on his side for a month, meaning his illness was prolonged, even if he was lying on his back. And it was said: ‘Imran was prevented by his illness from lying on his back, so he was ordered to pray on his side. So he has a choice, and lying on the back is preferable. And we have already mentioned the permissibility of facing whatever one can, and facing the qiblah is dropped due to the excuse of illness and the like. (And) if he prays lying on his back as is preferable, (he places a pillow under his head) or similar, (so that his face faces the qiblah, not the sky) and he becomes like a sitting person, so that he can gesture for bowing and prostration. For the reality of lying on the back prevents healthy people from gesturing with them, so how about the sick? (It is recommended) for the sick (to erect his knees if he can, so that he does not extend them) and stretch his legs (toward the qiblah) which is disliked for one able to avoid it, as in Majma‘ al-Riwāyāt and others. And (if gesturing) with his head (is not possible, the prayer is postponed) for a small number, which is the prayer of a day and night or less, by agreement. But if they become numerous and exceed one day and night (as long as he understands) the content (of speech), then he prays them in one narration. He said in al-Hidayah: That is correct, and the author of al-Kanz followed him in it. And in al-Mustasfā: He said: The correct view is that qada is not dropped because he apprehends the content of speech, so he is not like an unconscious person. End quote. And al-Kamāl ibn al-Humām (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Whoever reflects on the reasoning of the scholars in the principles for imposing qada for one who goes mad or loses consciousness for less than a day and night, not for more than one day and night, it becomes clear in his mind that qada is obligatory for this patient who is unable to gesture with his head up to one day and night, so that he must will it if he is able, but if it exceeds, it drops. End quote. So this verifier, Ibn al-Humām, tends toward the view of non-obligation merely due to understanding speech, if the inability exceeds one day and night, especially since the author of al-Hidayah explicitly stated, contrary to his own view, in his book al-Tajnīs wa al-Mazīd: Qada is dropped if his inability to gesture with his head continues for more than five prayers, even if he understands the content of speech. Indeed, he said contrary to his statement in al-Hidayah. The exact text in al-Tajnīs: The patient who reaches a state where he cannot pray, neither by gesture nor otherwise, and dies, nothing is required of him from the expiations of prayers, nor is he held accountable; because he was not able to perform prayer during life such that performance would be obligatory, nor does anything become incumbent as a substitute—i.e., the fidyah. If he recovers and becomes healthy, and the prayers he missed are less than one day and night, he makes them up because he was able to perform prayer at a time that has a substitute, so it becomes binding. But if they are more than one day and night, he is not obligated to make them up because no substitute becomes due. And Qāḍī Khān authenticated this, and similar in al-Muḥīṭ. And Shaykh al-Islam and Fakhr al-Islam chose it. And in al-Ẓahīriyya: It is the apparent riwāyah and the fatwā is upon it. And in al-Khulāṣa: It is the chosen view. And it was authenticated in al-Yanābī‘ and al-Badā’i‘, and al-Walwālijī asserted it, because it does not benefit since he was not able to perform, so he becomes like one unconscious. End quote. (And) al-Kamāl said: (Its like) i.e., like the authentication of Qāḍī Khān (in al-Muḥīṭ. And Shaykh al-Islam) Khwāhar Zādah (and Fakhr al-Islam) al-Sarakhsī (chose it) i.e., the dropping of prayers when many. End quote. And in al-Ẓahīriyya: It is the apparent riwāyah and the fatwā is upon it, as in Mi‘rāj al-Dirāyah, and in al-Bazzāziyya: If he is unable to gesture with the head, the chosen view is what Imam al-Sarakhsī said, that prayer drops. End quote. And in al-Fatāwā al-Sughrā: If unable to gesture with the head such that it is more than a day and night, prayer drops; if less, no. End quote. And in al-Khulāṣa: It is the chosen view, and it was authenticated in al-Yanābī‘—he said: it is correct, as in al-Tātārkhāniyya and al-Badā’i‘—and al-Walwālijī asserted it, saying: The patient who reaches a state where he cannot pray by gesture or otherwise, and dies, nothing is required of him from the expiation of prayer, nor is he held accountable. And in Sharḥ al-Ṭaḥāwī: If he is unable to gesture and move his head, the prayer drops, as in al-Tātārkhāniyya. And in Sharḥ al-Mukhtār: If he is unable to gesture with his head, the prayer is postponed, due to what we narrated—i.e., his statement: „The sick person prays standing; if he cannot, then sitting; if he cannot, then on his back, gesturing; if he cannot, then Allah is most entitled to accept his excuse.“ Then he said: If he dies in that state, nothing is upon him; if he recovers, the correct view is that he must make up a day and night only, meaning if his inability did not exceed a day and night. For if it exceeds, he does not have to make up anything, to avoid hardship, as in madness and unconsciousness, unlike sleep, for the sleeper makes them up even if many, because it usually does not exceed a day and night. End quote. And in Sharḥ al-Kanz by al-Dayrī: They differed on the meaning of his statement, peace be upon him: “Allah is most entitled to accept his excuse.” Those who do not say qada drops when unable to gesture say: The meaning is that Allah is most entitled to accept the excuse of delay, not dropping. And those who say it drops say: The meaning is that Allah is most entitled to accept the excuse of dropping. End quote. And you have known from what we mentioned that the one who chose dropping...
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the obligation of qada (making up missed prayers) for a patient who is unable to perform even the minimal gesture (ishara) with the head during prayer. The author begins by describing the recommended posture for a patient praying while lying on his back: he should place a pillow under his head to face the qiblah and bend his knees to avoid pointing them toward the qiblah. If gesturing is impossible, the prayer is postponed. The crux of the issue is whether a patient who remains unable to gesture for a prolonged period (more than a day and night, or more than five prayers) must make up the missed prayers upon recovery. Two opinions are presented: (1) The view of al-Hidayah that qada is required as long as the patient understands speech, making it analogous to wakefulness. (2) The view attributed to the same author in al-Tajnīs wa al-Mazīd and supported by many later scholars (Qadi Khan, al-Sarakhsi, etc.) that if the inability exceeds a day and night (or five prayers), qada is not obligatory. This latter view is deemed the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) and the basis for fatwa. The reasoning is that such a patient is akin to one unconscious (maghmi alayh) for a long period, where qada is also waived to avoid hardship. The text also discusses the interpretation of the hadith: 'Allah is most entitled to accept his excuse'—those opposing the dropping view interpret it as excusing delay; those supporting dropping interpret it as excusing the obligation entirely. The passage thus presents a detailed intra-madhhab analysis, with the preponderant position being that prolonged inability exempts one from qada.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-īmā'): Gesturing with the head or body to indicate bowing and prostration when unable to perform actual movements in prayer.
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Making up a missed obligatory prayer after its prescribed time has elapsed.
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The well-established and transmitted ruling from the early Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) as opposed to rare or weak narrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in this passage, what is the ruling for a patient who is unable to gesture with his head for a period of three days and then recovers?
A) He must make up all missed prayers.
B) He must make up only the first day and night of missed prayers.
C) He is not required to make up any missed prayers.
D) He must perform fidyah for each missed prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab's mufta bihi position is that prolonged inability to gesture (> day/night) drops the obligation of qada, analogous to prolonged unconsciousness.

Salah — Prayer
beginner

Pointing with Eye or Heart Does Not Substitute Head-Nodding in Prayer
Ruling on invalid substitutes for head-nodding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on substituting head-nodding in prayer and explain the textual and analogical proofs.

Core Idea
Pointing with eye or heart cannot substitute head-nodding; Allah accepts the excuse itself.

When a person is unable to perform the physical acts of bowing and prostration in prayer, Hanafi fiqh prescribes nodding (ima) with the head. But what if even nodding is impossible? This passage from the Hanafi tradition clarifies that no other gesture—such as pointing with the eye, heart, or eyebrow—can take its place. The ruling is supported by a Prophetic hadith and a clear analogy (qiyas). Let us examine the proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on what a person who cannot nod with his head should do: he does not point with his eye, heart, or eyebrow. The ruling is that these gestures are not valid substitutes.
The ruling applies to those who are unable to perform the usual ima.
2
Locate the textual proof
The author cites a hadith: 'If he cannot (nod), then Allah is more deserving of accepting his excuse.' This indicates that when nodding is impossible, no alternative action is required.
The hadith is a proof that Allah does not burden a person beyond capacity.
3
Trace the analogical proof
A qiyas is made: prostration is attached to the head, not to the eye, heart, or eyebrow. Just as prostration cannot be transferred to the hand, it cannot be transferred to these other parts. Therefore, pointing with them is invalid.
The illah (effective cause) is the specific attachment to the head.
4
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
While Hanafis hold that no substitute is prescribed, other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow pointing with the eyes or even the heart in extreme cases. The practical difference is whether the person is required to perform any gesture at all.
The Hanafi position is based on the hadith and qiyas; other schools may interpret the hadith differently or rely on other evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ عَجِزَ عَنِ الْإِيمَاءِ بِرَأْسِهِ لَمْ يُومِ بِعَيْنِهِ وَلَا بِقَلْبِهِ وَلَا بِحَاجِبِهِ؛ لِمَا رُوِّينَا مِنْ قَوْلِهِ - صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ -: فَإِنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ أَيِ الْإِيمَاءَ بِرَأْسِهِ فَاللَّهُ أَحَقُّ بِقَبُولِ الْعُذْرِ مِنْهُ وَلِأَنَّ السُّجُودَ تَعَلَّقَ بِالرَّأْسِ دُونَ الْعَيْنِ وَالْقَلْبِ وَالْحَاجِبِ، فَلَا يُنْقَلُ إِلَيْهَا كَالْيَدِ.
And whoever is unable to nod with his head, he does not point with his eye, nor his heart, nor his eyebrow; because of what we narrated from his statement - peace be upon him: 'If he cannot (nod with his head), then Allah is more deserving of accepting his excuse than him.' And because prostration is attached to the head, not to the eye, heart, or eyebrow, so it is not transferred to them, like the hand.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if a person is unable to nod (ima) with his head during prayer, he cannot substitute it by pointing with his eye, heart, or eyebrow. The author provides two proofs: first, a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicating that when nodding with the head is impossible, Allah accepts the excuse without requiring an alternative action. Second, analogy (qiyas): just as the act of prostration is specifically linked to the head and is not transferred to the hand, similarly, pointing with other parts is invalid because the original act (sujud/ima) is attached to the head and cannot be shifted to other body parts. The ruling is clear: the only valid substitution for inability to prostrate is nodding (ima) with the head, and if that is impossible, no substitute is prescribed.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-īmā'): Nodding with the head as a substitute for bowing and prostration in prayer for those unable to perform them.
الرأس (al-ra's): Head, the specific body part to which the acts of bowing and prostration are attached.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a person is unable to nod with his head during prayer?
A) He should point with his eye
B) He should point with his heart
C) He should point with his eyebrow
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Only head-nodding substitutes for bowing/prostration in Hanafi fiqh; pointing with other parts is invalid.

Praying While Unable to Bow and Prostrate: The Hanafi Ruling
Ruling and reasoning for inability to bow/prostrate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person who can stand but cannot bow or prostrate, and apply the ruling to variant scenarios involving wounds or incontinence.

Core Idea
Standing is a means to prostration; when the goal is impossible, the means drop.

When illness or injury prevents bowing and prostration, but standing is still possible, the Hanafi school provides a clear ruling. The default assumption might be to stand and nod, but the school holds that sitting and nodding is better and that standing itself is excused. This lesson unpacks the logical reasoning behind this ruling and applies it to real-life scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The text begins with a specific case: the person can stand but cannot bow or prostrate. The ruling is that he prays sitting with ima (nodding). Note that sitting and nodding is preferred over standing and nodding.
ima = nodding with the head as a substitute for ruku' and sujud.
2
Understand the rational proof (talil)
Standing is a means (wasilah) to achieve the goal of humility through bowing and prostration. Since the goal itself is impossible, the means are no longer obligatory. This is the core logical proof.
The author uses the principle that when the essential purpose (maqsud) is lost, what is below it (standing) is not obligatory.
3
Apply the rule to variant scenarios
The text then provides additional cases: if standing causes a wound to flow or incontinence, the person sits and performs actual bowing and prostration. But if even prostration causes the wound to flow, then he sits and nods. Similarly, weakness in recitation while standing does not excuse standing.
Notice the conditional logic: each scenario depends on what movements are possible without harm.
4
Note the ikhtilaf regarding congregation
The text reports a difference of opinion: if a person is unable to stand but can pray at home standing, should he go to the mosque? Some sources say he prays at home standing; others say he should go and pray sitting with ima. The author does not resolve this, indicating it remains a valid ikhtilaf.
This shows that fiqh is not always uniform; a mufti must consider the circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَدِرَ عَلَى الْقِيَامِ، وَعَجِزَ عَنِ الرُّكُوعِ وَالسُّجُودِ، صَلَّى قَاعِداً بِالْإِيمَاءِ، وَهُوَ أَفْضَلُ مِنْ إِيمَائِهِ قَائِماً؛ لِأَنَّ الْإِيمَاءَ قَاعِداً أَشْبَهُ بِالسُّجُودِ، لِكَوْنِ رَأْسِهِ فِيهِ أَخْفَضَ وَأَقْرَبَ إِلَى الْأَرْضِ. وَإِنَّمَا سَقَطَ عَنْهُ الْقِيَامُ، لِأَنَّ الْقِيَامَ وَسِيلَةٌ إِلَى السُّجُودِ، وَالْمَقْصُودُ الْخُضُوعُ وَالْخُشُوعُ لِلَّهِ تَعَالَى، وَإِنَّمَا يَحْصُلُ ذَلِكَ بِالرُّكُوعِ وَالسُّجُودِ، فَإِذَا فَاتَ الْمَقْصُودُ بِالذَّاتِ لَا يَجِبُ مَا هُوَ دُونَهُ. وَفِي «الْبَدَائِعِ»: يُسْقَطُ الرُّكُوعُ عَمَّنْ عَجِزَ عَنِ السُّجُودِ، وَإِنْ قَدِرَ عَلَى الرُّكُوعِ. وَإِذَا كَانَ بِهِ جُرَاحَةٌ إِنْ قَامَ سَأَلَ جَرْحُهُ، وَإِنْ قَعَدَ لَا يَسِيلُ، أَوْ كَانَ شَيْخاً كَبِيراً إِذَا قَامَ سَلَسَ بَوْلُهُ وَإِنْ قَعَدَ اسْتَمْسَكَ، يُصَلِّي قَاعِداً بِرُكُوعٍ وَسُجُودٍ. وَإِنْ كَانَ لَوْ سَجَدَ سَالَ أَيْضاً صَلَّى بِالْإِيمَاءِ قَاعِداً. وَكَذَا لَوْ ضَعِفَ عَنِ الْقِرَاءَةِ بِالْقِيَامِ، وَاخْتَلَفَ التَّصْحِيفُ فِيمَا لَوْ خَرَجَ إِلَى الْجَمَاعِةِ يَعْجِزُ عَنِ الْقِيَامِ، وَإِنْ صَلَّى فِي بَيْتِهِ صَلَّى قَائِماً صُحِّحَ. وَفِي «الْخُلَاصَةِ»: أَنَّهُ يُصَلِّي فِي بَيْتِهِ. وَفِي «الْوَلْوَالِجِيَّةِ» صُحِّحَ خِلَافُهُ، كَمَا فِي «التَّتَارْخَانِيَّةِ».
And if he is able to stand, but unable to bow and prostrate, he prays sitting with ima, and that is better than his ima while standing, because ima while sitting is more similar to prostration, since his head in that state is lower and closer to the ground. He is excused from standing because standing is a means to prostration, and the purpose is humility and submissiveness to Allah Almighty, and that is only achieved by bowing and prostration. So when the essential purpose is missed, what is below it is not obligatory. In 'al-Bada'i': bowing is dropped from one who is unable to prostrate, even if able to bow. And if he has a wound that flows if he stands but not if he sits, or he is an old man who becomes incontinent when standing but holds when sitting, he prays sitting with bowing and prostration. And if when he prostrates the wound also flows, he prays with ima while sitting. Similarly, if he is too weak to recite while standing, and there is difference in verification regarding going to congregation while unable to stand: if he prays at home standing, it is verified. In 'al-Khulasa': he prays at home. In 'al-Walwalijiyya', the opposite is verified, as in 'al-Tatarkhaniyya'.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the prayer of a sick person who can stand but cannot bow (ruku') or prostrate (sujud). The ruling: he should sit and nod (ima). The author explains why sitting and nodding is superior to standing and nodding: it resembles the actual posture of prostration more closely because the head is lower. Standing is removed because it is merely a means to the ultimate goal of humility through bowing and prostration; when the goal itself is unattainable, the means are not obligatory. Additional details from Bada'i': even if one can bow but not prostrate, bowing is dropped. Practical scenarios: if standing causes a wound to flow or incontinence, he sits and performs actual bowing and prostration, but if both standing and prostrating cause flow, he sits and nods. Weakness in recitation while standing does not excuse standing; however, if going to the mosque for congregation requires ability to stand, there is a difference of opinion. The author presents variant rulings from different Hanafi sources, indicating that the relied-upon position may vary depending on circumstances.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-īmā'): Nodding with the head as a substitute for bowing and prostration when unable to perform the actual movements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A sick person can stand but cannot bow or prostrate. How should he pray?
A) Stand and nod
B) Sit and nod
C) Sit and perform actual bowing and prostration if possible
D) Lie down and point with eyes

Key Takeaway
When unavoidable inability prevents ruku' and sujud, standing is also excused, and the prayer is performed sitting with ima.

Building (Binā') in Prayer When Ability Changes Mid-Prayer
Ruling on continuing prayer when ability changes during the prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building (binā') when a worshipper becomes unable to perform full actions mid-prayer, distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the later Hanafi authorities, and apply the conditions under which building is permitted.

Core Idea
Building is better than restarting: performing part with full actions is superior to doing all with ima.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses a common dilemma: a person starts praying with full ability but loses it mid-prayer. Should he continue as best he can, or start over? The Hanafi school, on its preferred view, permits 'building' (binā'), meaning you complete the prayer according to your current ability. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and the specific conditions that govern when building is and is not allowed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Scenario
The passage begins with a healthy person who falls ill during prayer. Determine the immediate ruling: he completes the prayer as able, even by nodding (ima).
2
Analyze the Proof (Talil)
The author gives a comparative argument: performing part of the prayer with full actions (ruku/sujud) and part with ima is better than doing the entire prayer with ima. Recognize this as an istihsan-based preference.
This is not a direct textual proof but a rational preference.
3
Explore the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Hanifa reportedly required restarting once ima becomes necessary, citing the obligation from the opening takbir. The author sides with the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that building is valid.
The mufta bihi view is building.
4
Understand Sub-Rulings on Building
The passage details when building is NOT allowed: (1) from ima back to full actions (building strong upon weak), and (2) from lying-down ima to sitting without bowing/prostration.
The principle: you cannot build a stronger action on a weaker one.
5
Apply the Principle to New Cases
Using the analogy of iqtida (following an imam), building is allowed when the change is from a stronger state to a weaker one (e.g., standing → sitting with ima), but not the reverse.
This ensures the prayer remains valid without unnecessary repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ عَرَضَ لَهُ أَيْ لِمَنِ افْتَتَحَ الصَّلَاةَ صَحِيحاً، ثُمَّ عَرَضَ لَهُ مَرَضٌ فِي صَلَاتِهِ، يُتِمُّهَا بِمَا قَدِرَ، وَلَوْ كَانَ الْإِتْمَامُ بِالْإِيمَاءِ فِي الْمَشْهُورِ، وَهُوَ الصَّحِيحُ؛ لِأَنَّ أَدَاءَ بَعْضِ صَلَاتِهِ بِرُكُوعٍ وَسُجُودٍ وَبَعْضِهَا بِالْإِيمَاءِ، أَوْلَى مِنْ أَنْ يُؤَدِّيَ الْكُلَّ بِالْإِيمَاءِ. وَعَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّهُ يَسْتَقْبِلُ إِذَا صَارَ إِلَى الْإِيمَاءِ، لِأَنَّ تَحْرِيمَتَهُ انْعَقَدَتْ مُوجِبَةً لِلرُّكُوعِ وَالسُّجُودِ، فَلَا تَجُوزُ بِدُونِهَا. وَالصَّحِيحُ الْبِنَاءُ لِمَا قُلْنَا. وَلَوْ صَلَّى الْمَرِيضُ قَاعِداً يَرْكَعُ وَيَسْجُدُ، فَصَحَّ، بَنَى؛ لِأَنَّ الْبِنَاءَ كَالِاقْتِدَاءِ، فَيَصِحُّ عِنْدَهُمَا، لَا عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ. وَفِي قَوْلِهِ صَلَّى إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ قَدِرَ قَبْلَ الرُّكُوعِ وَالسُّجُودِ بَنَى اتِّفَاقاً؛ لِعَدَمِ بِنَاءِ الْقَوِيِّ عَلَى الضَّعِيفِ. وَلَوْ كَانَ قَدْ أَدَّى بَعْضَهَا مُومِياً فَقَدِرَ عَلَى الرُّكُوعِ وَالسُّجُودِ، وَلَوْ قَاعِداً لَا يَبْنِي؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ بِنَاءِ الْقَوِيِّ عَلَى الضَّعِيفِ. وَلَوْ كَانَ يُومِي مُضْطَجِعاً، ثُمَّ قَدِرَ عَلَى الْقُعُودِ وَلَمْ يَقْدِرْ عَلَى الرُّكُوعِ وَالسُّجُودِ اسْتَأْنَفَ عَلَى الْمُخْتَارِ؛ لِأَنَّ حَالَةَ الْقُعُودِ أَقْوَى، فَلَا يَجُوزُ بِنَاؤُهُ عَلَى الضَّعِيفِ كَمَا فِي «التَّبْيِينِ».
And if a person who began the prayer in good health then falls ill during his prayer, he completes it as he is able, even if the completion is with ima in the well-known view, and that is correct; because performing part of his prayer with bowing and prostration and part with ima is better than performing the entire prayer with ima. And from Abu Hanifa: he starts over when he reaches the point of ima, because his opening takbir was bound to require bowing and prostration, so it is not valid without them. But the correct view is building, for what we said. And if a sick person prays sitting, bowing and prostrating, and then recovers, he builds; because building is like following (an imam), so it is valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), not according to Muhammad as previously. And in his saying 'prayed' is an indication that if he is able before bowing and prostration, he builds by agreement, because there is no building of the strong upon the weak. And if he had performed part of it pointing (while lying), then becomes able to bow and prostrate, even if sitting, he does not build; because that would be building the strong upon the weak. And if he was pointing while lying down, then becomes able to sit but not to bow and prostrate, he starts over according to the chosen view; because the sitting state is stronger, so building upon the weak is not permissible, as in 'al-Tabyin'.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a person starts praying in a healthy state (e.g., standing, bowing, prostrating) but becomes ill mid-prayer, losing the ability to perform some actions. The ruling: he should complete the prayer according to his current ability, even if it means shifting to ima (nodding). This is the preferred view (sahih) and is called 'building' (bina). The reasoning is that performing part of the prayer with full actions and part with ima is superior to restarting and doing the whole prayer with ima. Abu Hanifa is reported to hold that one should restart if ima becomes necessary, but the author sides with the building view. The passage then provides specific sub-rulings: If a sick person prays sitting with actual bowing and prostration and then recovers (becomes able to stand), he builds upon that prayer according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but not according to Muhammad's other view. If one recovers before bowing and prostration (i.e., in the standing part), building is agreed upon. If one performed part of the prayer with ima (e.g., lying down) and then gains the ability to bow and prostrate, he does not build but must restart, because that would be building a stronger state upon a weaker one. Similarly, if one was pointing lying down and then can sit but still cannot bow/prostrate, he does not build because sitting is stronger than lying down.

Key Terms
البناء (al-binā'): Continuing a prayer upon recovery or change in ability without starting over from the beginning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person begins praying standing, then becomes unable to bow/prostrate and must use ima. What should he do according to the correct view?
A) Start the prayer over
B) Complete the prayer with ima
C) Wait until he recovers
D) Abandon the prayer

Key Takeaway
If ability changes mid-prayer, building (binā') is preferred over restarting, except when building strong upon weak.

Qada for Insanity and Unconsciousness: Duration and Cause Matter
Expansion on qada ruling for insanity/unconsciousness with intra-madhhab debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for missed prayers due to insanity/unconsciousness, distinguish between the views of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the condition of continuous non-self-induced loss of consciousness.

Core Idea
Qada drops only after six missed prayer times, and only if the cause is a divine affliction, not self-induced.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah expands on the base ruling from Nur al-Idah regarding the obligation to make up prayers missed due to insanity (junun) or unconsciousness (ighma). The commentator clarifies that the ruling depends on both the duration and the cause of the loss of intellect. He presents proofs from the Companions and reveals an intra-Hanafi debate on how to count 'multiplicity' (kathra).

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its conditions
The matn states: if the loss of intellect covers five prayers or less, qada is wajib; if six or more, no qada. Note the condition that the cause must be a divine affliction (afa samawiyya) and continuous.
Insanity/unconsciousness from other causes (e.g., self-induced) has different rulings.
2
Understand the proofs from the Companions
The commentator cites narrations: Ibn Umar said a day and night requires qada; Ali made up four prayers; Ibn Umar did not make up when unconscious for more than a day and night. These establish the principle of a threshold.
Also analogies to a sleeping person (qada required for short periods) and a menstruating woman (no qada for long periods due to hardship).
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on counting multiplicity
Imam Muhammad counts by number of prayer times (six complete times needed to drop qada). Abu Yusuf (and a narration from Abu Hanifa) counts by hours (a full day and night). The commentator prefers Muhammad's view because 'multiplicity' involves repetition, which is linked to prayer times.
The practical difference: under Muhammad's view, missing 5.5 prayer times still requires qada; under Abu Yusuf, if the total hours exceed 24, qada drops.
4
Apply the qualification regarding self-induced causes and continuity
If loss of intellect is self-induced (wine, hashish, medicine), Abu Hanifa says qada is always wajib; Muhammad says if the substance is mubah (like medicine), qada drops like illness. Also, the loss must be continuous: if he regains consciousness at a known time, even briefly, it breaks the continuity and qada may be required again.
Fright from a wild animal or human is considered a divine affliction (due to heart weakness), so qada drops after the threshold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جن، أو أغمي عليه، خمس صلوات، قضى. ولو أكثر لا. قضى) ( ومنْ جُنَّ) بآفة سماوية، (أو أُغمي عليه، ولو بفزع من سبع أو آدمي فاستمر به (خمس صلوات، تلك الصلوات [ (وَلَو) كانت (أكثر ) بأن خرج وقت السادسة (لا) يقـضـي مـا فـاتـه مـن الصلوات؛ ] (١) لما عن ابن عمرة أنه قال في الذي يغمى عليه يوماً وليـلـة قـال: يقضي ). وعــن علي له أنه أغمي عليه أربع صلوات فقضاهن (۳). وابن عمر أغمي عليه أكـثـر مـن يــوم وليـلـة فـلـم يقض، ولأن المدة إذا قصرت لا يخرج في القضاء فيجب كالنائم، وإذا طالت يُحرج فيسقط كالحائض، والجنون، والإغماء، فيما رواه أبو سليمان وهو الصحيح، والكثرة تعتبر من حيث الأوقات عند محمد، حتى لا يسقط القضاء ما لم يستوعب ست صلوات، وعند أبي يوسف تعتبر من حيث الساعات، وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة، والأول أصح؛ لأن الكثرة بالدخول في حد التكرار، قيدنا زوال العقل بما ذكرنا، إذ لو زال عقله بالخمر يلزمه القضاء وإن طال؛ لأنه حصل بما هو معصية، يوجب التخفيف ولهذا يقع طلاقه، وكذا إذا ذهب عقله بالبنج أو الدواء عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن سقوط القضاء عرف بالأثر إذا حصل بآفة سماوية، فلا يقاس عليه ما حصل بفعله، وعـنـد محمـد يسقط لأنه مباح فصار كالمريض، ولو أغمي عليه بفزع من سبع أو آدمي لا يـجـب عـلـيـه الـقـضـاء بالإجماع؛ لأن الخوف بسبب ضعف قلبه وهو مرض، وقيدنا بدوام الإغماء؛ لأنه إذا كان يفيق في وقت معلوم نحو أن يخف عند الصبح فيفيق قليلاً، ثم يعاوده الإغماء، يعتبر الإفاقة فيبطل ما قبلها من حكم الإغماء إذا كان أقل من يوم وليلة، وإن لم تكن لإفاقته وقت معلوم، إلا أنه يتكلم بغتة بكلام الأصحاء ثم يُغمى عليه، فلا عبرة بهذه الإفاقة كذا في « التاتارخانية».
And whoever becomes insane (by a heavenly affliction) or unconscious, even by fright from a wild animal or a human, and it continues with him, (must make up) five prayers. And if more, no. (qada) (and whoever becomes insane) by a heavenly affliction, (or unconscious) (five prayers) those prayers (and if) they are (more) i.e., the time of the sixth has entered, (no) he does not make up what he missed of prayers; because of what is narrated from Ibn Umar that he said concerning one who is unconscious for a day and a night: he said: he makes up (the prayers). And from Ali that he was unconscious for four prayers and he made them up. And Ibn Umar was unconscious for more than a day and a night and did not make them up. And because when the duration is short, he is not excused from making up, so it is obligatory as for a sleeping person; and when it is long, he would be in difficulty, so it falls away as for a menstruating woman. And insanity and unconsciousness, according to what Abu Sulayman narrated and it is the correct view. And multiplicity is considered in terms of prayer times according to Muhammad, so that qada is not dropped unless it encompasses six prayers. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is considered in terms of hours, and this is a narration from Abu Hanifa. And the first is more correct because multiplicity enters into the limit of repetition. We restricted loss of intellect to what we mentioned, for if his intellect was lost due to wine, he must make up even if long, because it occurred through a sin, which requires [lit. necessitates] leniency, and for this reason his divorce takes effect. Similarly, if his intellect goes due to hashish or medicine according to Abu Hanifa, because dropping of qada is known by [legal] effect when it occurs by a heavenly affliction, so what occurs by his own action cannot be analogized to it. And according to Muhammad, it falls away because it is permissible, so it becomes like a sick person. And if he is unconscious due to fright from a wild animal or a human, qada is not obligatory upon him by consensus, because fear is due to weakness of his heart and it is an illness. And we restricted [the ruling] to continuous unconsciousness; because if he regains consciousness at a known time, such as being light at dawn and regains consciousness briefly, then unconsciousness returns, the period of consciousness is considered and it invalidates what preceded it from the ruling of unconsciousness if it is less than a day and a night. And if his consciousness has no known time, except that he suddenly speaks with the speech of healthy people and then becomes unconscious, this consciousness is not regarded, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (Imdad al-Fattah) expands on the matn ruling regarding the obligation of making up missed prayers (qada) for one who becomes insane (junun) or unconscious (ighma) due to a divine affliction (afa samawiyya). The base ruling states: if the loss of consciousness covers five prayers or less, qada is wajib; if it covers six or more, qada is not required (sāqit). The commentator first clarifies that the insanity must be from a divine affliction, and unconsciousness even from fright (faza') — whether from a wild animal or human — follows the same rule. He then provides proofs: narrations from Ibn Umar (that a day and night requires qada) and Ali (four prayers made up), and Ibn Umar's practice of not making up for more than a day and night. The reasoning is that short periods do not excuse one from qada, analogized to a sleeping person (who must make up), while long periods cause hardship and qada falls, analogized to a menstruating woman (who does not make up missed prayers). The passage then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad considers 'multiplicity' (kathra) by number of prayer times — i.e., six complete prayer times must be missed for qada to drop. Abu Yusuf (and a narration from Abu Hanifa) considers it by the actual hours (i.e., a full day and night without return of consciousness). The commentator prefers Muhammad's view because 'multiplicity' involves repetition (takrār) which is linked to prayer times. He then addresses cases where loss of intellect is self-induced (by wine, hashish, or medicine): Abu Hanifa says qada is wajib even if long because it was a sin or a voluntary act, not a divine affliction; Muhammad argues that if the substance is permissible (mubah, like medicine) then qada drops, analogized to illness. The passage concludes with a qualification: continuous unconsciousness is required for the ruling; if he regains consciousness at a known time for even a brief period, it breaks the continuity and qada may be required again, unless the return is momentary and unexpected (no known time), in which case it is disregarded.

Key Terms
جنون (junūn): Loss of intellect due to a divine affliction (āfa samāwiyya); specifically excludes self-induced intoxication.
إغماء (ighmā'): Unconsciousness, whether from illness, fright, or other causes; ruled like junūn in terms of qada.
آفة سماوية (āfa samāwiyya): A heavenly affliction, i.e., a cause beyond human control like illness or accident, as opposed to self-induced acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (Muhammad), how many missed prayers are required for qada to drop due to insanity/unconsciousness?
A) Three prayers
B) Five prayers
C) Six prayers
D) Twelve prayers

Key Takeaway
Qada for missed prayers due to insanity/unconsciousness is waived only if the duration exceeds five prayer times (six or more) and the cause is a divine affliction, continuous, and not self-induced.

When Must a Dying Person Make a Will for Missed Prayers and Fasts?
Rulings on when a dying person must make a will for missed prayers and fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that make a will for fidyah obligatory for missed prayers and fasts according to the Hanafi school, and apply the rule to different scenarios of ability and excuse.

Core Idea
A will for missed religious acts is only required when the person was capable of performing them but did not; inability excuses.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses a critical end-of-life legal concern: when must a dying person make a will for compensation (fidyah) of missed prayers and fasts? The Hanafi school makes a careful distinction based on the person's ability to perform the act and the nature of the excuse. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper estate planning and fulfilling the deceased's obligations to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the person's ability
For prayers: determine if the sick person was able to pray even by gesture (ima') during their illness. For fasts: determine if the traveller/sick person broke fast with a valid excuse (travel/illness) and whether they had an opportunity to make up the days before death.
The key distinction is between the person having an opportunity to perform the obligation and not doing so, versus having no opportunity at all.
2
Apply the hadith principle of acceptance of excuse
The hadith 'If he cannot, then Allah is more deserving of accepting the excuse from him' is interpreted by the Hanafis either as allowing delay (tajwiz al-ta'khir) or dropping (isqat). In either case, if there was no ability or opportunity, no will is required.
The hadith is cited as proof for the rule that inability lifts the obligation of making a will.
3
Distinguish between prayers and fasts
For prayers: if unable to pray even by gesture, no will is required, regardless of the number. For fasts: if the person broke fast with a valid excuse (travel/illness) and then died before having an opportunity to make up, no will. But if the person broke fast without a valid excuse, or had a valid excuse but later became able to make up (e.g., recovered from illness) yet did not, then a will for fidyah is obligatory.
The difference arises because the obligation of prayers is stricter; fidyah is not accepted for missed prayers at all, whereas it is accepted for missed fasts under these conditions.
4
Understand the executor's role and the one-third limit
If a will is made, the executor (wali) must carry it out from one-third of the deceased's estate. This is because during illness, the dying person's right to dispose of wealth is limited to one-third, and the heirs' right attaches to the remaining two-thirds.
This rule applies to many financial obligations like zakat al-fitr, expiations, etc., as mentioned at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فصل في إسقاط الصلاة والصوم وغيرهما
( إذا مات المريض، ولم يقدر على أداء ( الصَّلاة بالإيماء ) برأسه (لا يلزمه الإيصاء بها، وإن قلت)، بأن كانت دون ست صلوات، كما لو كثرت، لما رويناه من قوله : « فَإِنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ فَاللَّهُ أَحَقُّ بِقَبُولِ العُدْرِ مِنْهُ (۱) لأن القائل بأن معنى قبول العذر جواز التأخير، لا يقول بلزوم القضاء، إلا بإدراك زمن يسعه ولم يوجد، والقائل بأن معنى قبول العذر الإسقاط ظاهر في عدم لزوم الإيصاء بما لم يجب عليه (وَكَذَا) حكم (الصوم) في شهر رمضان ( إنْ أفطر فيه المسافر والمريض، وماتا قبل الإقامة) للمسافر، (وَ) قبل (الصَّحَّةِ) للمريض، لأن رمضان في حقهما كشعبان في حق غيرهما، لا يلزمهما صيامه، ولم يدركا عدة من أيام أخر للقضاء، فليس عليهما الوصية بشيء، (وَ) لـزم (عليه) يعني: على من أفطر في رمضان ولو بغير عذر الوصيَّةُ بما ) أي : بفدية ما (قَدَرَ عليه) مـن إدراك عدة من أيام أخر، لو كان فطره لمرض أو سفر، وبقدر ما أفطره بغير عذر، وإن لم يدرك عدة من أيام أخر للزوم الصوم عليه في وقته، والتقصير مضاف إليه فيرجى العفو عنه بفضل الله؛ لإيصائه بفدية ذلك الذي قدر عليه، وبقي بذمّتِهِ) حتى أدركه الموت، وأوصى بفدية ما عليه من صيام فرض رمضان، وكذا صوم كفارة يمين، وقتل خطأ، وظهار ) ، وجناية على إحرام"، وقتل محرم صيداً، أو صوم منذور، فيُخْرِجُ عنهُ وَلِيُّهُ) أي: من له ولاية التصرف في ماله بوراثة، أو وصاية فيلزمه إخراجـه من ثلث ما ترك الموصي؛ لأن حقه في ثلث ماله حال مرضه، وتعلق حق الوارث بالثلثين، فلا ينفذ قهراً على الوارث إلا في الثلث، وعلى هذا دين صدقة الفطر، والنفقة الواجبة، والخراج، والجزية، والكفارات المالية ) ، والوصية بالحج، والصدقة المنذورة، والاعتكاف المنذور عن صومه؛ لاعــن
Chapter on the dropping (isqat) of prayer, fasting, and other things. If a sick person dies and was not able to perform prayer even by gesture (i.e., by nodding his head), he is not obliged to make a will (wasiyya) regarding it, even if few (prayers), similarly fasting, if the traveller and the sick person broke the fast (in Ramadan) and died before returning to residence (for the traveller) and before recovery (for the sick), then upon him is the will for what he was able (to make up) and remained in his obligation, so his executor (wali) brings it out from one-third of what he left. Chapter on the dropping of prayer, fasting, and other things. (If the sick person dies and was not able to perform) prayer (by gesture) with his head, (he is not obliged to make a will regarding it, even if few), i.e., less than six prayers, just as if many, because of what we narrated from his (the Prophet's) saying: 'So if he cannot, then Allah is more deserving of accepting the excuse from him.' Because the one who says that the meaning of 'accepting the excuse' is permission to delay does not affirm the obligation of qada, unless he reached a time that could accommodate it and it did not exist. And the one who says that the meaning of 'accepting the excuse' is dropping (isqat) is clear in the non-obligation of making a will for what was not obligatory upon him. (And similarly) the ruling (of fasting) in the month of Ramadan (if the traveller and the sick person break the fast in it, and they die before) return to residence (for the traveller) (and before) recovery (for the sick person), because Ramadan in their regard is like Sha'ban in the regard of others; it is not obligatory for them to fast it, and they did not reach a number of other days to make up, so there is no will upon them for anything. (And) it is necessary (upon him) meaning: upon the one who broke the fast in Ramadan, even without an excuse, (the will for what) i.e., with the fidyah of what (he was able) to reach a number of other days, if his breaking was due to illness or travel, and to the extent of what he broke without excuse, even if he did not reach a number of other days, because fasting was obligatory on him in its time, and the falling short is attributed to him, so forgiveness is hoped for by Allah's grace due to his making a will for the fidyah of that which he was able (to make up). (And it remained in his obligation) until death overtook him, and he made a will for the fidyah of what was upon him of fasting of the obligatory Ramadan, and likewise fasting of expiation for an oath, accidental killing, dhihar, and crime against ihram, and killing of game by a muhrim, or a vowed fast. Then his executor brings it out, i.e., the one who has authority to dispose of his wealth by inheritance or executorship, and he must bring it out from one-third of what the testator left, because his right is in one-third of his wealth during his illness, and the right of the heir is attached to two-thirds, so it is not enforced against the heir except in the one-third. And upon this (basis) are the debts of sadaqat al-fitr, obligatory maintenance, kharaj, jizya, and financial expiations, and the will for hajj, vowed charity, and vowed i'tikaf (seclusion) for fasting; because...
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (Imdad al-Fattah) explains the rulings of the chapter (fasl) on 'isqat' — the legal mechanisms for dropping or compensating missed religious obligations that remain unfulfilled at death. The chapter specifically addresses the obligation of making a will (wasiyya) for missed prayers and fasts. The commentator first states the matn rule: if a sick person dies who was unable to pray even by gesture (ima'), he is not required to make a will for those prayers, regardless of whether the number missed was small (less than six) or large. The proof is the hadith: 'If he cannot, then Allah is more deserving of accepting the excuse from him,' which is interpreted either as permission to delay (tajwiz al-ta'khir) — requiring qada only if there was time — or as dropping (isqat) entirely. In either case, no will is required because the obligation was not firmly established. The same rule applies to fasting: a traveler or sick person who broke the fast in Ramadan and died before making it up (due to continued travel or illness) has no obligation to make a will, because Ramadan was not obligatory for them in that state — like Sha'ban for a healthy resident. However, for a person who broke the fast without a valid excuse (e.g., deliberately), or who had a valid excuse but was able to make up some days (i.e., he recovered or ended travel for some time), he must make a will for fidyah (compensation) for those days he was able to make up but did not. If he died before making up, the will for fidyah is obligatory and is executed by his executor (wali) from one-third of his estate, because the right of the heirs is in two-thirds, and the testator's right during illness is limited to one-third. The same principle applies to other obligations like expiatory fasts (for oath, accidental killing, dhihar, ihram violations, hunting in ihram), vowed fasts, and also financial obligations like zakat al-fitr, maintenance (nafaqah), land tax (kharaj), jizya, and financial expiations, as well as will for hajj and vowed charity and i'tikaf.

Key Terms
إيصاء (īṣā'): A will or testament stating that fidyah (compensation) should be given on behalf of the deceased for missed religious obligations.
فدية (fidya): Monetary compensation for missed obligatory acts (like fasting) that cannot be made up after death; amount is typically feeding one poor person per missed day.
ولي (walī): Executor or guardian who has authority to manage the deceased's wealth and carry out the will; can be an heir or appointed executor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a dying person required to make a will for fidyah of missed fasts?
A) If he missed any fasts, regardless of reason
B) Only if he missed fasts due to travel or illness and died before making them up
C) If he was able to make up the missed fasts (e.g., after recovery) but did not, and then died
D) Only if he missed fasts intentionally

Key Takeaway
A will for fidyah is only obligatory for missed fasts if the person had the ability to make them up and failed to do so; for prayers, if unable to perform even by gesture, no will is required.

No Bequest Required for Missed Prayers/Fasts if Unable to Perform
Ruling with proof: no bequest for missed acts if unable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequest for missed prayers and fasts when the deceased was unable to perform them, and explain the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
No bequest required for missed prayers or fasts if death occurred before ability to perform.

This passage addresses a critical question for the terminally ill: must they bequeath missed acts of worship? The Hanafi school answers clearly: if the person was unable to perform even by gesture, there is no duty to bequeath. The ruling is supported by a hadith that emphasizes Allah's acceptance of valid excuses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and context
The passage addresses two scenarios: a sick person unable to pray by gesture, and a traveler or sick person who broke Ramadan fasts and died before making them up.
The inability is extreme—cannot even gesture for prayer.
2
State the ruling
In both cases, no bequest (īṣāʾ) is required for the missed acts. The text explicitly says 'لا يلزمه الإيصاء بها'.
This applies regardless of whether missed acts are few or many.
3
Locate the dalīl
The proof is a hadith: 'فَإِنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ فَاللَّهُ أَحَقُّ بِقَبُولِ العُدْرِ مِنْهُ' (If he cannot, Allah is more worthy of accepting the excuse from him).
The hadith is narrated as gharīb but accepted in Hanafi sources.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalālah
The hadith indicates that if one cannot perform the act, there is no blame. The commentary offers two interpretations: (1) accepting excuse means permission to delay, but since no time was available, no obligation; (2) accepting excuse means dropping the obligation entirely.
Both interpretations lead to the same conclusion: no bequest.
5
Note the scope
The ruling applies to prayers and fasts. For other obligations, different rules may apply (e.g., zakāh is still due from estate if owed).
The passage states 'وكذا الصوم'—the same applies to fasting.

فصل في إسقاط الصلاة والصوم وغيرهما
( إذا مات المريض، ولم يقدر على أداء ( الصَّلاة بالإيماء ) برأسه (لا يلزمه الإيصاء بها، وإن قلت)، بأن كانت دون ست صلوات، كما لو كثرت، لما رويناه من قوله : « فَإِنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ فَاللَّهُ أَحَقُّ بِقَبُولِ العُدْرِ مِنْهُ (۱) لأن القائل بأن معنى قبول العذر جواز التأخير، لا يقول بلزوم القضاء، إلا بإدراك زمن يسعه ولم يوجد، والقائل بأن معنى قبول العذر الإسقاط ظاهر في عدم لزوم الإيصاء بما لم يجب عليه (وَكَذَا) حكم (الصوم) في شهر رمضان ( إنْ أفطر فيه المسافر والمريض، وماتا قبل الإقامة) للمسافر، (وَ) قبل (الصَّحَّةِ) للمريض، لأن رمضان في حقهما كشعبان في حق غيرهما، لا يلزمهما صيامه، ولم يدركا عدة من أيام أخر للقضاء، فليس عليهما الوصية بشيء،
Section on dropping (fulfillment) of prayer, fasting, and others. If a sick person dies and was not able to perform prayer by gesture, it is not necessary for him to bequeath it, even if few. Likewise for fasting, if the traveler and the sick broke (the fast) in Ramadan and died before (the traveler's) completion of journey and (the sick's) health, and upon him is the bequest for what he was able, and it remains in his liability, so his guardian pays from one-third of what he left. Section on dropping of prayer, fasting, and others. (When a sick person dies and was not able to perform prayer by gesture with his head, it is not necessary for him to bequeath it, even if few), meaning even if less than six prayers, similar to if they were many, due to what we narrated from his saying: 'For if he cannot, then Allah is more worthy of accepting the excuse from him.'
